### AT: Taiwan !\-\--China LIO\-\--2NC

#### No LIO impact\-\--the US is just as bad, China accepts broad order, and its worst impulses cannot be deterred. 

**Mueller 21**, Political Scientist, Ohio State University, Senior
Fellow, CATO Institute, quoting Yun Jiang, Researcher, Australian
National University (John Mueller, May 18, 2021, "China: Rise or
Demise?" CATO Policy Analysis, No. 917,
https://www.cato.org/sites/cato.org/files/2021-05/policy-analysis-917.pdf)

Most importantly, [[Xi wants to overcome]{.underline}]{.mark} what he
and other [[Chinese]{.underline}]{.mark} view as [past
[humiliations]{.mark}]{.underline}---ones going back to the opium war of
1839. Primarily, the Chinese want [to be treated with
respect]{.underline} and deference.186 That scarcely seems to present a
threat, and to a considerable degree, it seems sensible for other
countries, including the United States, to accept, and even service,
such vaporous and cosmetic goals. The United States, after all,
continually declares itself to be the one indispensable nation. If the
United States can wallow in such self-important, childish, essentially
meaningless, and decidedly fatuous proclamations, why should other
nations be denied the opportunity to emit similar inconsequential
rattlings? If China yearns for self-absorbed pretensions about being a
big player, that should be of little concern---and its success rate is
unlikely to be any better than that of the United States. A sardonic
crack by Yun Jiang, a researcher at the Australian National University,
may put the issue in perspective: ["[China wants what other]{.mark}
great [powers do]{.mark}. It wants [to follow]{.mark} international
[rules]{.mark} and norms [when]{.mark} it is [in its interest, and
disregard]{.mark} rules and norms [when]{.mark} the [circumstances suit
it]{.mark}."]{.underline}187

[However, [China]{.mark} actually [has]{.mark} had [a]{.mark} reasonably
[good record]{.mark} of [complying with]{.mark}]{.underline} complaints
brought against it in [the [W]{.mark}orld [T]{.mark}rade
[O]{.mark}rganization]{.underline}, a trajectory that can be encouraged
by wise policy.188 [It has [also]{.mark}]{.underline} abandoned
undervaluing its currency to boost growth while [[reducing its]{.mark}
weighted mean [tariff rate]{.mark}]{.underline} from 30 percent in 1992
to less than 4 percent in 2017.189 Notes Fingar, "In the early years,
acquiescence to the rules of [the [liberal order]{.mark}]{.underline}
was grudging at best, but [pragmatic]{.underline} as [[accommodation
became]{.mark} more [common]{.mark}]{.underline} as maintaining access
and relationships became ever more critical [[for China's]{.mark}
sustained [growth]{.mark}]{.underline}. . . . China is more prosperous,
more powerful, more influential. But it is also more deeply enmeshed in
and dependent on the rules-based international order, more vulnerable to
developments and decisions beyond its borders, and more constrained by
citizen expectations and demands."190 Suggests Ho-fung Hung, "[China is
[far from]{.mark} becoming [a subversive power]{.mark} that will
transform the existing neoliberal order [because China]{.mark} itself
[is one of the]{.mark} biggest [beneficiaries]{.mark} of this
order]{.underline}."191 Similarly, Shambaugh points out that:

[China [has become]{.mark} a [better]{.mark}]{.underline} "global
citizen" [[in]{.mark} almost [all areas of]{.mark} global
[governance]{.mark}]{.underline} (in many cases halting its previously
noncompliant behavior). China has become much more [deeply involved in
UN [peacekeeping]{.mark} operations]{.underline}, international
[disaster relief, [anti-piracy]{.mark}]{.underline} operations in the
Gulf of Aden, global [public health,
[c]{.mark}ounter[t]{.mark}errorism]{.underline}, law enforcement,
[[non-prolif]{.mark}eration, [climate]{.mark} change]{.underline} and
environmental cooperation, [global economic governance]{.underline},
overseas development assistance (ODA), [financial contributions to the
UN]{.underline} operating budget, [and other areas]{.underline}.192

In some of his recent writings, Minxin Pei seems to suggest that U.S.
policy should seek to exacerbate China's mostly self-inflicted pain.193
However, that does not seem particularly wise or necessary. As the
Brookings Institution's Strobe Talbott put it for the Soviet Union, the
system there went "into meltdown because of inadequacies and defects at
its core, not because of anything the outside world had done or
threatened to do." Cold War historian Westad agrees: it came about
primarily "because of weaknesses and contradictions in the Soviet system
itself."194 One maxim often attributed to Napoleon was "never interrupt
your enemy when he's making a mistake." It is not at all clear that it
is sensible to think of China as an "enemy."195 However, the arresting
dictum does seem to have some bearing on the case.

At the same time, fulminations that seem to suggest a desire for "regime
change" in China are unlikely to be either wise or effective, not the
least because, as Lyle Goldstein points out, they can be used against
genuine indigenous efforts to promote human rights, and they founder on
the fact that "the United States manages to quietly and yet very
actively cooperate with states that have even worse human rights
records, such as Saudi Arabia."196

Overall, in fact, [the [record suggests]{.mark} that the [U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates]{.underline}, in part because it [has [often
supported]{.mark} countries with [appalling human rights]{.mark}
records]{.underline}, has not been very successful at influencing
foreign regimes on the treatment of their own citizens. After 1975,
democracy did grow remarkably [[in Latin America]{.underline}]{.mark},
but [the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates]{.underline} mostly stood back,
watching and encouraging, and perhaps leading by example. It [did [use
force]{.mark}]{.underline} successfully [[to reimpose
democracy]{.underline}]{.mark} when it lapsed in Grenada and Panama. But
it seems more reasonable to suggest that the efforts struck a responsive
chord rather than that the United States created one. Thus,
American-armed efforts to install, or re-install, democracy in those two
countries lasted, while similar ventures earlier had failed to do so
after the American troops left. The impressive advance in democracy (and
capitalism) in countries in East Central Europe that escaped the Soviet
embrace after 1989 did not require much participation by the United
States. The chief role model, as had been the case for Spain and
Portugal in the mid-1970s, was that supplied by the open, productive,
and prosperous countries in Western Europe, not by the United States.
The newly liberated countries were attracted to the European Union and
NATO---two clubs they could join if they came up to standards. For the
most part, they were quite willing to try.197

Nor is a policy of "containment" likely to be successful. It is commonly
argued that containment worked against the Soviet Union. In the long
run, policymakers hoped that the Soviets, frustrated in their
expansionist drive, would become less hostile and more accommodating. In
the end, however, the problem that containment was designed to deal with
went away only when the policy itself lapsed---a development that helped
Communism to self-destruct and that led directly to the end of the Cold
War when an exhausted and much overextended Soviet Union abandoned its
expansionist ideology.198 In 1975, three countries---Cambodia, South
Vietnam, and Laos---abruptly toppled into the Communist camp. Then,
partly out of fear of repeating the Vietnam experience, the United
States went into a sort of containment funk: it simply watched from the
sidelines as the Soviet Union opportunistically gathered a set of Third
World countries into its imperial embrace: Angola in 1976, Mozambique
and Ethiopia in 1977, South Yemen and Afghanistan in 1978, and Grenada
and Nicaragua in 1979. The Soviets at first were quite gleeful about
these acquisitions---the "correlation of forces," as they called it, had
decisively and most agreeably shifted in their direction. However,
almost all the acquisitions soon became economic and political basket
cases, fraught with dissension, financial mismanagement, and civil
warfare, and turned expectantly to the Soviet Union for maternal warmth
and sustenance. Most disastrous for the Soviet Union was its experience
in Afghanistan, where it engaged in a long, enervating war. Meanwhile,
the Soviet empire in Eastern Europe had also became a severe economic
drain and a political liability for Moscow. In the end, the economic
bill to the Soviet Union for maintaining its growing collection of
dependencies around the world rose dramatically.199 It soon came to
realize that it would have been better off contained.

Thus, [it is far from clear what the United States]{.underline} and the
rest of the world [can do about China's economically foolish
authoritarian drive]{.underline} except to issue declarations of
disapproval and to deal more warily. That's pretty much what it did
under the regime of Mao Zedong during China's massively self-destructive
periods of the Great Leap Forward of 1958--62 and of the Great
Proletarian Cultural Revolution of 1965--75.200 Both of these were
eventually altered by policy change within the Communist Party rather
than by internal rebellion or by outside pressure. As Bandow points out,
"[[No]{.mark} combination of]{.underline} lectures, sanctions, and
[[threat]{.mark}s [is likely to force a nationalistic regime to abandon
policies]{.mark} that [it views]{.mark} as [essential for its]{.mark}
political [control]{.mark}]{.underline}."201

#### China benefits from the order, is genuinely pragmatic, and isn't monolithically opposed. 

**Fravel et al. 19**, \*Professor of Political Science, Massachusetts
Institute of Technology, Ph.D., Stanford University, \*\*Distinguished
Scholar, Wilson Center, \*\*\*Senior Fellow, Carnegie Endowment for
International Peace, \*\*\*\*Senior Fellow, Yale Law School's Paul Tsai
China Center, \*\*\*\*\*Professor Emeritus, Harvard University (\*M
Taylor Fravel, \*\*J. Stapelton Roy, \*\*\*Michael D. Swaine,
\*\*\*\*Susan A. Thornton, \*\*\*\*\*Ezra Vogel, July 3, 2019, "China is
not an Enemy," Washington Post Opinion,
https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/making-china-a-us-enemy-is-counterproductive/2019/07/02/647d49d0-9bfa-11e9-b27f-ed2942f73d70_story.html)

2\. We do not believe Beijing is an economic enemy or an existential
national security threat that must be confronted in every sphere; [[nor
is China a monolith]{.mark}, or [the views of]{.mark} its [leaders set
in stone]{.mark}. Although its]{.underline} rapid economic and military
[growth has led Beijing toward a more assertive international
role]{.underline}, many [[Chinese officials and]{.mark} other [elites
know]{.mark} that [a]{.mark} moderate, [pragmatic]{.mark} and genuinely
cooperative [approach with]{.mark} the [West serves China's
interests]{.mark}. Washington's [adversarial stance]{.mark}]{.underline}
toward Beijing [[weakens]{.mark} the influence of [those voices in favor
of]{.mark} assertive [nationalists]{.mark}]{.underline}. With the right
balance of competition and cooperation, U.S. actions can strengthen
those Chinese leaders who want China to play a constructive role in
world affairs.

3\. U.S. efforts to treat China as an enemy and decouple it from the
global economy will damage the United States' international role and
reputation and undermine the economic interests of all nations. U.S.
opposition will not prevent the continued expansion of the Chinese
economy, a greater global market share for Chinese companies and an
increase in China's role in world affairs. Moreover, the United States
cannot significantly slow China's rise without damaging itself. If the
United States presses its allies to treat China as an economic and
political enemy, it will weaken its relations with those allies and
could end up isolating itself rather than Beijing.

4\. The [[fear]{.mark} that [Beijing will replace]{.mark} the
[U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates as the global leader [is exaggerated.
Most]{.mark} other [countries have no interest in such]{.mark} an
[outcome]{.mark}]{.underline}, and it is not clear that Beijing itself
sees this goal as necessary or feasible. Moreover, a government intent
on limiting the information and opportunities available to its own
citizens and harshly repressing its ethnic minorities will not garner
meaningful international support nor succeed in attracting global
talent. The best American response to these practices is to work with
our allies and partners to create a more open and prosperous world in
which China is offered the opportunity to participate. Efforts to
isolate China will simply weaken those Chinese intent on developing a
more humane and tolerant society.

5\. Although China has set a goal of becoming a world-class military by
midcentury, it faces immense hurdles to operating as a globally dominant
military power. However, Beijing's growing military capabilities have
already eroded the United States' long-standing military preeminence in
the Western Pacific. The best way to respond to this is not to engage in
an open-ended arms race centered on offensive, deep-strike weapons and
the virtually impossible goal of reasserting full-spectrum U.S.
dominance up to China's borders. A wiser policy is to work with allies
to maintain deterrence, emphasizing defensive-oriented, area denial
capabilities, resiliency and the ability to frustrate attacks on U.S. or
allied territory, while strengthening crisis-management efforts with
Beijing.

6\. [Beijing]{.underline} is seeking to weaken the role of Western
democratic norms within the global order. But it [is not seeking to
overturn vital]{.underline} economic and other [components of that
[order]{.mark} from which [China]{.mark} itself [has benefited]{.mark}
for decades]{.underline}. Indeed, China's engagement in the
international system is essential to the system's survival and to
effective action on common problems such as climate change. The United
States should encourage Chinese participation in new or modified global
regimes in which rising powers have a greater voice. [A
[zero-sum]{.mark} approach]{.underline} to China's role [would [only
encourage Beijing to]{.mark} either disengage from the system or
[sponsor a divided]{.mark} global [order]{.mark} that would be damaging
to Western interests.]{.underline}

#### China can't become a military hegemon AND it's structurally constrained. 

**Latham 23**, Professor of International Relations, Macalester College,
Senior Fellow, Defense Priorities, Washington D.C.-based think tank
(Andrew Latham, April 6, 2023, "China: A great power but not a
superpower," The Hill,
https://thehill.com/opinion/international/3936751-china-a-great-power-but-not-a-superpower/)

But [[China]{.mark} today [falls far short of superpower]{.mark}
standing]{.underline}. To be sure, China dominates its home region
economically and exercises considerable influence farther afield. But
[[it lacks]{.mark} the [ability to project military]{.mark} power
[beyond its]{.mark} immediate [hinterland, is facing]{.mark} a
[concerted effort to balance it]{.mark}]{.underline} diplomatically and
[[militarily across]{.mark} the Indo-[Pacific]{.mark}]{.underline}
region, [[has]{.mark} almost [no soft power]{.mark}]{.underline}
resources to exploit, [[is not]{.mark} the [hub of a]{.mark}
globe-spanning [alliance system and remains]{.mark} more [a
rule-taker]{.mark} than rule-maker in the global institutional
space]{.underline}.

All this being the case, [the conclusion is undeniable: While China
is]{.underline} unquestionably both [a regional power]{.underline} and a
great power, when it comes to [superpower status, it]{.underline} simply
[doesn't make the cut]{.underline}.

[[Nor]{.underline}]{.mark}, it must be said, [[is it likely to
ascend]{.mark} to the status of superpower any time soon. [China's
population is]{.mark} both getting [old and shrinking]{.mark}; its
[economy looks]{.mark}]{.underline} increasingly like those of other
countries that have come [to be [mired]{.mark} in the]{.underline}
so-called ["middle income trap"; [and]{.mark} its]{.underline} once
bright [geopolitical star has begun to fade as
[countries]{.mark}]{.underline} around the world begin to [[take steps
to balance against]{.underline}]{.mark} what they see [as [a]{.mark}n
increasingly [belligerent]{.mark} great [power]{.mark}.]{.underline}

To be sure, there are those who look at the evidence and come to
different conclusions. But to most serious China watchers, the trend
lines have become obvious. [[China
is [peaking](https://theconversation.com/rethinking-the-us-china-fight-does-china-really-threaten-american-power-abroad-148672)]{.underline}]{.mark} ---
and is doing so long before assuming the mantle of superpower.

And if this is the case -- [if China is something less than a full-blown
superpower -- then the whole edifice of]{.underline} Cold War II and its
derivative [[Containment]{.mark} 2.0 [is]{.mark}]{.underline} revealed
for what it is: [[a]{.mark} nostalgia-inflected [delusion fraught
with]{.mark} the danger of [overreaction]{.mark}]{.underline}, needless
geopolitical friction and possibly even tragically unnecessary war.

#### China can't become a military power. 

**Stanley and Swaine 22**, \*Advocacy Director of the Quincy Institute
for Responsible Statecraft, Ph.D., Public Policy, \*\*Director, Quincy
Institute East Asia Program, Ph.D., Government, Harvard University
(\*Marcus Stanley, \*\*Michael Swaine, June 16, 2022, "Stop using the
China 'threat' to throw more money at the Pentagon," Responsible
Statecraft,
https://responsiblestatecraft.org/2022/06/16/stop-using-the-china-threat-to-throw-more-money-at-the-pentagon/)

First, although in recent years China has become more motivated to build
up its military capacities, [[there is no]{.underline}]{.mark} clear
[[evidence]{.mark} that [China is]{.mark}]{.underline} currently
[[determined to supplant]{.mark} the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates
[as]{.mark} the [dominant]{.mark} military [power
globally]{.mark}]{.underline}. This might emerge in the future as a
deliberate Chinese policy, but it has not yet. 

In this context, it's critical to understand
that [[China does not possess]{.mark} and is not currently investing to
create a [worldwide]{.mark} network of [military
bases]{.mark}]{.underline} like the United States. [China now [spends
far less than]{.mark} the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates does
[on]{.mark} its [military]{.mark}]{.underline} --- a little over
one-third of the amount in absolute terms, and less than half as much as
a share of its economy.  [China also [does not have extensive]{.mark}
military [alliances]{.mark}]{.underline} across the globe, as the United
States does.

Second, [China is [still focused]{.mark} mainly [on its]{.mark}
immediate [neighborhood, where it seeks to]{.mark}]{.underline} end
America's past military predominance in order to [[reduce its]{.mark}
own [vulnerabilities]{.mark}]{.underline}. In this effort, China has met
with real success. U.S. maritime and air superiority in the Western
Pacific is largely gone and is almost certainly not coming back in any
foreseeable time frame, absent the unlikely collapse of China's
economy. There is no "silver bullet" technology or level of resources
that can easily alter this picture.   

Pentagon reports have highlighted significant near-peer level PLA
advances in five specific realms: shipbuilding, electronic warfare,
ballistic and cruise missiles, cyber capabilities, and integrated air
defense systems. As noted, these efforts are primarily directed toward
negating U.S. military superiority along China's coasts and nearby
maritime areas, especially around Taiwan.  

Chinese strategy has also shifted beyond immediate offshore defense to
protection of the "open seas," in order, [it
says](http://www.xinhuanet.com/english/download/whitepaperonnationaldefenseinnewera.doc),
to "safeguard national sovereignty, protect strategic sea lines of
communication, and participate in international maritime cooperation."
Nowhere has it said or even implied that China's ultimate and necessary
goal is to become the dominant global military power. It wants to create
a "world-class military" that has the capabilities of other advanced
militaries. 

Any effort to spend our way back to total dominance in the Western
Pacific near China's shores is likely doomed to failure. China spends
less than 2 percent of its total economic product, or GDP, on defense,
as opposed to the almost 4 percent (and rising) that the United States
spends. That means the Chinese have room to ramp up military spending,
possibly to match our own. China also does not have to spread its
defense spending around the entire world as the United States does, but
instead focuses mainly on the Western Pacific.

Not only will an arms race with China for dominance in Asia fail because
it can very likely keep up with us financially, but an open-ended arms
race will be extremely expensive and risky. A race for superiority
off China's shores would increase regional instability and
increase the risk of disastrous miscalculation, possibly leading to
direct military conflict between the U.S. and China.   

But crucially, [the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [can achieve]{.mark}
its [defensive goals in]{.mark} the Western
[Pacific, including]{.mark} the defense of [Taiwan, without]{.mark}
trying to spend our way to total military [dominance]{.mark}
of the Western Pacific]{.underline}. Maintaining a defensive presence
adequate to deter Chinese aggression is far less expensive than seeking
dominance, and can be achieved well within our current budget
trajectory. 

Although [the [Chinese]{.mark} military]{.underline} has largely
closed the gap with the United States in the Western Pacific, it [[still
faces]{.mark} many strategic, [structural, tech]{.mark}nological, [and
manpower constraints in developing]{.mark} into a [superior]{.mark}
military [force]{.mark}]{.underline} (or even a full-fledged peer
competitor) to the United States. In some cases, [[it is decades
behind]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### They can't project power globally. 

**Bekkevold 23**, Senior China Fellow, Norwegian Institute for Defense
Studies, former Norwegian Diplomat (Jo Inge Bekkevold, March 2, 2023,
"How the United States, China, and the former Soviet Union stack up."
Foreign Policy,
https://foreignpolicy.com/2023/03/02/china-superpower-us-new-cold-war-rivalry-geopolitics/)

[[China]{.underline}]{.mark}, however, [[is only a regional
power]{.underline}]{.mark}. It wields global economic power and
influence, but [[the]{.mark} geographic [reach of its military
is]{.mark} largely [limited to]{.mark} the Asian and Indo-[Pacific
theaters]{.mark}]{.underline}. From its position in the East Asian
rimland, [China [has]{.mark} more [limited]{.mark} geographic
[reach]{.mark}]{.underline} into the Eurasian continent than the Cold
War-era Soviet Union did---and less access to the high seas than either
the United States or the Soviet Union. [The [U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates']{.underline} blessed geographic [[position gives
it]{.mark} direct and [unhindered access to the]{.mark} Atlantic,
[Pacific]{.mark}, and Arctic oceans]{.underline}. Although the Soviet
Union's access to the high seas was more restricted than that of the
United States, it still had direct access from its homeland to the
Pacific and Arctic oceans, as well as almost-but-not-quite direct access
to the Atlantic Ocean. [[China]{.mark} only borders the Pacific
Ocean---and [is]{.mark} largely [hemmed]{.mark} in [by]{.mark} major
[island chains it does not control]{.mark}.]{.underline}

If China is not satisfied being a regional power and aims to become a
true superpower, it will need to leapfrog the geopolitical constraints
of its home region.

Naturally, China's nuclear, space, and cyber capabilities all have
worldwide reach. China is modernizing and expanding its nuclear force as
well as its nuclear delivery platforms. Moreover, with nearly 600
satellites in orbit---of which 229 are intelligence, surveillance, and
reconnaissance satellites---China has the world's second largest fleet
of satellites after the United States. Chinese cyber capabilities can
wreak havoc in every corner of the world. And these days, balloons
should perhaps also be added to the picture as a potent capability with
global reach.

Nevertheless, [[the]{.mark} geographic [reach]{.mark} of]{.underline}
these [technologies [is insufficient to]{.mark} strongly
[influence]{.mark} strategic [theaters beyond Asia]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Even in an age of space and cyber warfare, coercive diplomacy is more
effective when troops can be physically deployed to a border or shore.
Gunboat diplomacy still requires boats, and airplanes still need
airfields to operate in far-flung regions. In other words, [[geography
makes China]{.mark} even more [dependent]{.mark} than the United
States]{.underline} and former Soviet Union [[on]{.mark} overseas [bases
and]{.mark} willing [allies]{.mark} to move its armed
forces]{.underline} beyond its home region. [[China has]{.mark} almost
[none of either]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[China]{.underline} currently [has only one overseas
base]{.underline}---its naval facility [in Djibouti]{.underline} staffed
with 400 Chinese marines. While the U.S. Navy sails the world's oceans
on a daily basis, [the Chinese navy only occasionally conducts naval
diplomacy missions beyond the Indo-Pacific]{.underline}. This may
change, of course, with the Chinese navy now adding aircraft carriers
and other blue-water vessels to its fast-growing fleet. China is
reportedly looking to add more overseas bases in the Pacific Ocean and
Africa. However, [[it is]{.mark} still [a long-term project for China to
turn]{.mark} itself [into a]{.mark} true
[superpower]{.mark}]{.underline} with world-wide military reach.
Moreover, the [[U.S.]{.mark} forward [posture in]{.mark} China's
[neighborhood complicates such]{.mark} a scenario]{.underline}---which
is where the third concept, regional hegemony, comes in.

#### China [won't]{.underline} and [can't]{.underline} establish a sphere of influence and it's [circumventable]{.underline} even if they did. 

**Mueller 21**, Political Scientist, Ohio State University, Senior
Fellow, CATO Institute, quoting Yun Jiang, Researcher, Australian
National University (John Mueller, May 18, 2021, "China: Rise or
Demise?" CATO Policy Analysis, No. 917,
https://www.cato.org/sites/cato.org/files/2021-05/policy-analysis-917.pdf)

A [Chinese Sphere of Influence?]{.underline}

Many commentators have raised concerns about China's often-expressed
desire to have more "influence" in the world. They point, in particular,
to an elaborate Chinese scheme---the Belt and Road Initiative---to
establish sea and land lanes to maintain and enhance China's ability to
trade with the rest of the world. Unsurprisingly, many in the United
States envision this as a key part of a diabolical plot by the Chinese
to "rule the world."46

However, it is absurd to obsess over something like the vacuous, if
venerable, sphere of influence concept. The notion that world affairs
are a process in which countries scamper around the world seeking to
establish spheres of influence is at best decidedly unhelpful and at
worst utterly misguided.47 But the concept continues to be embraced in
some quarters as if it had some palpable meaning. For example, in 2017,
the National Intelligence Council opined that "geopolitical competition
is on the rise as China and Russia seek to exert more sway over their
neighboring regions and promote an order in which U.S. influence does
not dominate."48

However, it is difficult to see whether American "influence" could be
said to "dominate" anywhere. For example, on December 21, 2017, when the
United States sought to alter the status of Jerusalem, the United
Nations General Assembly repudiated the U.S. stand in a nearly unanimous
vote that included many U.S. allies. That's influence?49 Indeed, it is
impressive that the hegemon, endowed with what Reich and Lebow aptly
call a grossly disproportionate military capacity, has had such a
miserable record of military achievement since 1945.50 The militarized
application of American primacy and hegemony to order the world has
often been a fiasco.51

The same holds for China's efforts. As Fingar notes, "muscular [displays
of [Chinese]{.mark} military [power]{.mark} may have been [intended to
dissuade]{.mark} neighboring [countries from lending support to]{.mark}
imputed [U.S.]{.mark} military planning]{.underline}, but they seriously
undercut efforts to reassure other countries that they had no reason to
fear China's 'peaceful rise.'"52 Westad points out that the efforts
"have all backfired: East Asia is much warier of Chinese aims today than
it was a decade ago," and he cites a Pew Research Center poll that shows
the percentage of South Koreans who viewed China's rise favorably fell
from 66 percent in 2002 to 34 percent in 2017.53

The Spread of Chinese Ideology

H. R. McMaster, a national security adviser in the Trump administration,
warned in 2020 that "China has become a threat because its leaders are
promoting a closed, authoritarian model as an alternative to democratic
governance and free-market economics."54 However, concerns that China is
bent on spreading some sort of dangerous alternative ideology seem to be
singularly misplaced. Not only are authoritarianism, dictatorships, and
kleptocracy far from new, but they can scarcely be said to constitute an
ideology.55 And insofar as they can, most of the countries so
"influenced" are unlikely to need much guidance: they are already
masters of the form.56 Moreover, as Harvard's Alistair Ian Johnston
notes, it is not all that clear that China is even trying to export or
promote its system: in Latin America, for instance, it has closer
economic and institutional ties with democratic Costa Rica and Chile
than with authoritarian Venezuela and Cuba.57

Actually, any comparative advantage the Chinese derive may stem less
from their own efforts at image projection than from the destructive and
appalling failures of American foreign and particularly military policy
over the past few decades, most notably in the Middle East.58 China may
also derive some reputational benefit from its comparatively successful
handling of the COVID-19 pandemic---although mainly from those who
ignore the successes of far less authoritarian regimes in the area, such
as Singapore, Taiwan, and South Korea.

The Contest to Police the Global Commons

[[China does seem]{.mark} to be more [assertive about]{.mark}
controlling piles of rocks]{.underline}, sometimes known as [[islands,
in]{.mark} the [S]{.mark}outh [C]{.mark}hina [S]{.mark}ea]{.underline}
and establishing a greater presence in that area. As Ambassador Chas
Freeman points out, "[[China has not expanded its]{.mark} maritime
territorial [claims]{.mark}, which [date back to]{.mark} at least
[the]{.mark} early [20th century]{.mark}]{.underline}," but [[it
disputes]{.mark} the [occupation of]{.mark} some of [those]{.mark}
claims by Malaysia, the Philippines, and Vietnam]{.underline} that
started in the 1970s. In the meantime, it has "transformed its tenuous
holdings in the South China Sea into an impressive array of artificial
islands and installations from which no other claimants can hope to
dislodge it."59

China's concerns seem to be two-fold. First, [the [sea lanes are crucial
to]{.mark} the country]{.underline}---fully [two-thirds of the
[commerce]{.mark}]{.underline} that flows through that waterway
originates in or [is destined for China. [Thus, China is likely]{.mark}
to be [worried occasionally]{.mark}]{.underline} about whether it can
count on the continuous benevolence of the U.S. Navy, which has
unilaterally presented itself to be the policing agency for what it
likes to call "the global commons"---a fancy term for oceans.60 After
all, policing agencies in cities not only keep the streets open and the
traffic flowing but also deem it necessary to close off some avenues
from time to time. [Second, [as China has become]{.mark} more
[prosperous]{.mark}, the [demand]{.mark} at home [for fish]{.mark} has
dramatically [increased]{.mark}]{.underline}, a phenomenon unlikely to
taper off as the country continues to develop. Consequently,
[[disputes]{.mark} with its neighbors [over fishing]{.mark} areas have
[increased]{.mark}]{.underline}.61

[[To deem these]{.mark} developments to constitute [some]{.mark} sort of
[global threat is excessive. Should China]{.mark} come to]{.underline}
imagine that it [[control]{.mark}s [that]{.mark} body of [water]{.mark},
it [will still have]{.mark} an [intense interest in]{.mark} the [free
flow of ships]{.mark} through it. [Even if China blocked]{.mark}
passage, [ships could take other routes]{.mark}]{.underline} with only
fairly minor inconvenience and expense.62 Zakaria notes a similarity
with the Monroe Doctrine of 1823 when the United States, then a rising
country, "declared the entire Western Hemisphere off-limits to the great
powers of Europe."63 Moreover, fishing disputes have been around forever
and are more nearly the inspiration for farce than for cosmic sturm und
drang.

#### China wouldn't establish one and US allies prevent total influence.

**Bo 20**, Opinion Columnist, SCMP, Retired CISS Senior Fellow, Tsinghua
University, former Senior Colonel, People's Liberation Army (Zhou Bo,
November 6, 2020, "A stronger China has no reason to seek a sphere of
influence even as US power wanes," South China Morning Post,
https://www.scmp.com/comment/opinion/article/3108284/stronger-china-has-no-reason-seek-sphere-influence-even-us-power)

But no international laws prohibit land reclamation, and some other
claimants have done the same. [[China maintains]{.underline}]{.mark}
that around [[100,000 ships transit]{.mark} through [the S]{.mark}outh
[C]{.mark}hina [S]{.mark}ea every year [without f]{.mark}reedom
[o]{.mark}f [n]{.mark}avigation [problems]{.mark}]{.underline}. What
China opposes are military activities against the interests of the
littoral states in the name of freedom of navigation.

[The [Chinese]{.mark}]{.underline} government has [[repeatedly
stressed]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[China]{.mark} would [not seek to
be]{.mark} a [hegemon even if it]{.mark} became [developed]{.mark}.
Although [this might sound like lip service]{.mark}]{.underline} to
some, [[its assertion is backed by]{.underline}]{.mark} history. For
[[2,000 years]{.underline}]{.mark}, much of [East [Asia was part
of]{.mark} the [Chinese sphere]{.mark} of influence, [but
that]{.mark}]{.underline} influence [[was primarily
cultural]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Admiral Zheng He's seven voyages in the Indian Ocean showed the sweeping
power of the "Celestial Empire" in the Ming dynasty, but the Chinese
didn't bother establishing a single military base in any of these
places. It was only 600 years later that the People's Liberation Army
established a [logistics
base](https://www.scmp.com/news/china/diplomacy-defence/article/2113300/chinas-djibouti-military-base-logistics-facility-or?module=inline&pgtype=article) there
in support of counter-piracy efforts in the Indian Ocean.

Influence and a sphere of influence are two different things. Today,
China's influence almost overlaps with that of the United States. Such
influence will grow further since China is widely expected to surpass
the US to become the world's largest economy in terms of gross domestic
product in 10 to 15 years. In other words, a global China is already
influential enough to demand any spheres of influence.

This then invites two most important questions for the 21st century: how
will the world accommodate China's rise? And, what can China bring to
the world?

China's [Belt and Road
Initiative](https://multimedia.scmp.com/news/china/article/3007692/belt-and-road/index.html) might
provide an answer to the second question. The initiative is ambitious,
but it is not a gilded instrument of a new Chinese order, as The
Economist asserted. It is not charity -- China invests for mutual
benefit. Neither is it a "debt trap" -- who would spend [trillions of
dollars](https://www.scmp.com/economy/china-economy/article/3093218/belt-and-road-initiative-debt-how-big-it-and-whats-next?module=inline&pgtype=article) to
lay such a mega trap? Any such grand scheme might take generations to
finish. But the projects are, day by day, changing the economic
landscape of the developing countries along the belt and road for the
better.

Contrary to Allison's suggestions, the last thing the US wants is to
cede any sphere of influence to China, its primary competitor in what it
sees as a [new era of great-power
competition](https://www.whitehouse.gov/wp-content/uploads/2017/12/NSS-Final-12-18-2017-0905.pdf).
In East Asia, the challenge is how Beijing and Washington, with their
overlapping influence, could coexist.

The [[US suspects China]{.mark} is [trying to drive it
out]{.mark}]{.underline} of the region. [It has [stepped up]{.mark} its
provocations [in]{.mark}]{.underline} the [[Taiwan]{.mark}
Strait [and]{.mark} the [S]{.mark}outh [C]{.mark}hina
[S]{.mark}ea]{.underline}, which risk testing the patience of a
PLA [growing ever
stronger](https://www.scmp.com/news/china/politics/article/3107686/policy-meeting-ends-defiant-note-chinese-leadership-insists?module=inline&pgtype=article).
﻿

Winston Churchill once famously quipped: "There is only one thing worse
than fighting with allies, and that is fighting without them." In East
Asia, [[America's allies are]{.mark} steeling themselves and [tiptoeing
between]{.mark} their [ally and]{.mark} their [top trading
partner]{.mark}]{.underline}. So far, none has joined the US Navy on
its freedom of navigation operations in the 12-nautical-mile waters off
Chinese rocks and islands in the South China Sea.

There is no guarantee the US could win in a military conflict with China
in the first chain of islands stretching from Japan to the Philippines
and the South China Sea. But should it lose, the consequence is not
moot: it would lose prestige and credibility among its allies and
partners in the region. The alliance would fall apart and it may have to
go home. In that sense, only the United States can displace the United
States in the Western Pacific.

Allison is right to conclude the illusion that other nations will simply
take their assigned place in a US-led international order is over. But
even if that means there are indeed [[spheres]{.mark} of
influence]{.underline} in the world today, China should beware and stay
away from them. They [[look more like]{.mark} perilous [traps
than]{.mark} power [vacuums awaiting China]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### An equal and opposite explanation exists for every instance of aggression\-\--it's impossible to give monocausal explanation of them as crazy Chinese warmongering. 

**Heer 22**, Distinguished Fellow at the Center for the National
Interest and a Non-Resident Senior Fellow at the Chicago Council on
Global Affairs (Paul Heer, February 11, 2022, "What Is Really Driving
Chinese Aggression?" National Interest,
https://nationalinterest.org/feature/what-really-driving-chinese-aggression-200516?page=0%2C1)

Foreign [[commentary on China]{.mark}'s]{.underline} international
[behavior]{.underline} routinely [[characterizes it]{.underline}]{.mark}
(in varying measures) [[as aggressive]{.underline}]{.mark}, coercive,
[expansionist]{.underline}, and nationalistic---especially [under
Chinese Communist Party]{.underline} (CCP) leader Xi Jinping. This is
usually attributed to the inherent nature of the CCP and/or of Xi
personally. Although the adjectives are often appropriate, [[what
is]{.mark} frequently [overlooked]{.mark}]{.underline} or dismissed
[[is]{.mark} the extent to which [Beijing is responding
to]{.mark}]{.underline} external stimuli or [[other players'
actions]{.underline}]{.mark}. Indeed, [[in virtually every case]{.mark}
of Chinese assertiveness or belligerence]{.underline}, it is not hard to
identify what Beijing felt the need to react to.

For example, [Chinese saber-rattling]{.underline} and ultimatums
[regarding [Taiwan]{.mark} are]{.underline} in part [[a]{.mark}
predictable [response to]{.mark}]{.underline} cumulative [actions by
both [Taipei]{.mark} and Washington that have [retreated
from]{.mark}]{.underline} or eroded [[prior]{.mark} historical
[understandings]{.mark}]{.underline} with Beijing [[about]{.mark} what
["one China"]{.mark} means]{.underline} and doesn't mean. CCP leaders
have long been prepared to postpone their pursuit of unification as long
as those understandings were operative.

Beijing's ongoing [crackdown in [Hong Kong was]{.mark}]{.underline}
prompted in part [by the [local authorities'
failure]{.mark}]{.underline}---after more than twenty years---[[to
enact]{.mark} national [security legislation]{.mark} (as required by
Hong Kong's Basic Law)]{.underline} and to maintain public order in the
wake of protests against such legislation and in favor of democratic
electoral reforms. Some protesters had become violent, demanded faster
political reform than Beijing had promised, rejected potential paths to
compromise, or openly advocated Hong Kong's independence. [[CCP]{.mark}
leaders]{.underline}, who had earlier been relatively patient, brought
the hammer down when they [[saw]{.mark} their control and Chinese
[sovereignty at risk]{.mark}. Similarly]{.underline}, Beijing's ruthless
crackdown and tactics [in [Xinjiang]{.mark}]{.underline}, although an
extreme overreaction, [were [triggered]{.mark}]{.underline} in part
[[by]{.mark} a series of [terror]{.mark}ist [attacks]{.mark} by Islamic
extremists]{.underline}.

There is also a reactive and defensive element to Beijing's aggressive
behavior in all of its territorial disputes---in which China has not
been the only assertive and nationalistic player. [In the [S]{.mark}outh
[C]{.mark}hina [S]{.mark}ea, the [action-reaction]{.mark} cycle that
[led]{.mark} over time [to Beijing's]{.mark} massive]{.underline}
reinforcement of its infrastructure and [military [presence]{.mark} has
[also featured]{.mark} efforts by [rival claimants]{.mark}]{.underline}
to bolster their own positions while similarly resisting any constraints
on their freedom of action. In the East China Sea, [Beijing's
increased]{.underline} operational [[presence]{.mark} over
the]{.underline} past decade [[was driven]{.underline}]{.mark} in part
[[by Tokyo's "nationalization" of]{.mark} the disputed [Senkaku]{.mark}
Islands]{.underline} in conjunction with an apparent retreat from what
some scholars assert was prior Japanese acknowledgment of the existence
of a territorial dispute and willingness to shelve it. Finally, [the
[U.S. view]{.mark} of aggressive Chinese behavior [on]{.mark} the
[Sino-Indian border is]{.mark}]{.underline} largely [[driven by
Indian]{.mark} tactical [reports]{.mark}, whereas Chinese accounts of
"who started it" might be no less credible]{.underline}.

In each of these territorial disputes, it is worth noting that it
probably was not in China's best interests to unilaterally provoke
tensions---unless Beijing in each case was intentionally looking for an
excuse to reinforce its military position at the expense of its
diplomatic reputation. It is more likely that CCP leaders, preoccupied
with myriad domestic challenges---including perennial fears of internal
unrest---preferred (as they frequently assert both publicly and
privately) to maintain a peaceful external environment that allows them
to focus on their domestic priorities. Precisely for that reason,
Beijing has long prepared to keep its territorial disputes and
aspirations on the back burner. But when other disputants take steps
that alter the equation in ways that challenge or erode China's
position, the Chinese feel compelled to react, and often do so
disproportionately.

Of course, in all of these cases---including Taiwan, Hong Kong, and
Xinjiang---there have been good reasons for the United States to be
sympathetic to parties on the receiving end of China's coercive
behavior. When Beijing assaults human rights or acts aggressively
toward---or scores points against---U.S. allies and partners, Washington
will appropriately take its stand. At the same time, an accurate and
objective assessment of such situations and formulation of the most
effective U.S. response would consider the underlying interactive
dynamics. It is not unreasonable for Beijing to expect or at least to
seek that other countries have some understanding of its perspective and
the circumstances under which it is acting. U.S. moral or material
support for the targets of Chinese assertiveness should not come at the
expense of recognizing what and how actions by those other players---and
Washington itself---contributed to Beijing's behavior. At the very
least, disregarding the Chinese point of view will hinder mutual
understanding and a constructive response to disputes and crises. On the
contrary, it usually reinforces Beijing's belligerent tendencies (as we
are now seeing with Moscow on the Ukraine issue).

China's "wolf warrior diplomacy" amply reflects this reactivity. It is
true, as has been widely noted over the past few years, that many
Chinese diplomats have grown increasingly vituperative in their
rhetoric, especially but not exclusively toward the United States. But
this has been reactive in two respects. First, it has partly been a
response to an increase in vitriolic comments about China by foreign
diplomats, most notably former Secretary of State Mike Pompeo, who also
mirrored (if not prompted) Beijing's resort to misinformation when
Washington and Beijing were trading blame for the spread of Covid-19.
Some Chinese diplomats apparently thought they should give as much as
they got in terms of polemics.

More importantly, "wolf warrior diplomacy" almost certainly has
reflected Beijing's view that its perspective on international issues
has been disregarded, particularly by Washington. Chinese diplomats
routinely protest what Beijing sees as challenges to China's
sovereignty, security, and stability and the lack of reciprocity and
accommodation in U.S.-China relations. But under the Trump
administration, and so far under the Biden administration, they have
frequently seen their complaints dismissed as invalid, self-serving,
exaggerated, or aimed at blaming Washington for bilateral tensions.
Those U.S. criticisms are often well-grounded, and Beijing has often
been similarly dismissive of American talking points. But clearly, both
sides have some valid concerns about each other, and both sides escalate
the rhetoric when they perceive that the other is rebuffing their
concerns.

This aspect of Beijing's "wolf warrior diplomacy" was recently explained
by scholars who examined the transcripts of Chinese Foreign Ministry
speeches over the past two decades.
They [found](https://www.washingtonpost.com/politics/2021/05/12/chinas-wolf-warrior-diplomats-like-talk-tough/) that
rhetorical hostility increased along with---and usually in response
to---growing external criticism of China, which eventually prompted
calls by Xi to enhance China's defensive "fighting spirit" in answer to
foreign "scolding." Beijing has thus been reacting to what it sees as
international disdain of its point of view and its expression of Chinese
interests and concerns. To whatever extent those interests and concerns
are legitimate, Beijing's frustration is predictable---even when its
response is arrogant and obnoxious.

Researchers at the International Crisis Group (ICG) highlighted the
reactive component of Chinese international behavior nearly a decade ago
when
its [analysis](https://www.crisisgroup.org/index.php?q=asia/north-east-asia/china/china-takes-gloves) coined
the term "reactive assertiveness" to characterize China's actions in the
South and East China Seas. ICG defined this as "responding forcefully to
perceived provocations," or when "Beijing uses an action by another
party as justification to push back hard and change the facts on the
ground in its favor." Although this was an accurate description, over
time, the narrative of Chinese "assertiveness" eclipsed the "reactive"
part of the equation in most foreign commentary, perhaps because Beijing
was deemed to have only "perceived" provocations and was so blatant in
"pushing back hard." There has also been little inclination in
Washington to assign any blame to smaller parties when China's behavior
and relative weight usually make it easy to portray Beijing as the bad
guy. Consequently, despite scholars' efforts
to [refute](https://academic.oup.com/cjip/article/7/1/47/574393) its
historical validity, China's "new assertiveness" became the prevailing
meme.

But the reactive element of Chinese behavior has always been
instrumental, and acknowledging it is crucially important. Beijing goes
on the offensive not just when it feels the urge or sees the opportunity
but when it perceives the need. Even the CCP's recent abandonment of
Deng Xiaoping's longstanding guidance for China to "hide its
capabilities and bide its time" was as much a response to Beijing's
assessment of an increasingly problematic and even hostile external
environment, as it was the result of a calculation that China had
greater capabilities with which to pursue its interests and security.
That combination has set the stage for the CCP's increasingly forceful
reactions to what it genuinely views as challenges by other players to
China's security and sovereignty and disregard for its strategic
perspective.

Again, none of this is meant to excuse Beijing's aggressive and coercive
behavior, or to absolve it of China's ample share of the blame for
tensions along its periphery. The CCP leads a nasty authoritarian regime
and is ruthless in its exercise of power both domestically and abroad.
And even when Beijing has valid international concerns, it routinely
overplays its hand or resorts to the bluntest policy instruments and the
least subtle forms of diplomacy. Nonetheless, strategic empathy---which
is wholly distinct from sympathy---remains crucial to understanding
Chinese behavior and to de-escalating crises, and averting conflict.
Within that framework, actions by other players---including China's
neighbors and the United States---are key drivers of Beijing's
perception of the international environment and responses to it. Failure
or refusal to recognize this can only lead to flawed diagnoses of the
challenge China represents and flawed prescriptions for dealing with it.

Many ask [[why Beijing behaves]{.mark} so [badly]{.mark}
internationally]{.underline}, and in ways that have been
counterproductive to its interests and reputation by sparking backlash
from other countries. It [[is not]{.underline}]{.mark} just [[because it
is in the CCP's nature]{.mark} to do so]{.underline}. It is primarily
because [[Chinese leaders, like]{.mark} national [leaders elsewhere, see
challenges]{.mark} and]{.underline} even [threats [to their]{.mark}
country's [interests]{.mark}, security, or
sovereignty]{.underline}---beyond the risks that they perceive to the
CCP regime's control and tenure. [We [should not be surprised if Beijing
acts in defense]{.mark} and pursuit of China's national
interests]{.underline}, and sometimes attributes its need to do so to
the actions of other countries. Mutual understanding and constructive
relations between the United States and China will be very difficult to
achieve if Washington overlooks or denies the defensive and reactive
component of Beijing's strategic mindset.

### AT: Taiwan !\-\--Structurally Constrained\-\--2NC

#### Xi won't aggress because he believes China's problems can be overcome\-\--when power does peak, peaceful decline would result, especially because of conventional deterrence. 

**Roy 22**, Senior Fellow, East-West Center, Honolulu, Ph.D., Political

[Rather than]{.underline} Zhongnanhai [secretly believing China has

[domestic]{.mark} economic, demographic and environmental [obstacles to

[[Observers]{.mark} who have characterized]{.underline} several recent

[[If it anticipated]{.mark} an impending period of [waning]{.mark}
relative [power]{.mark}]{.underline}, demonstrably [[risk-averse Beijing
would]{.mark} be inclined to [avoid]{.mark} entering that
period]{.underline} under the disadvantage of having recently

[action]{.mark}]{.underline}. 

Throughout history, Chinese governments have presented themselves as
cooperative rather than confrontational when they assessed that the
configuration of international forces was not favorable to prevailing
through the use of force.

Most recently, Deng Xiaoping and Jiang Zemin suppressed their own
nationalist impulses because they believed a clash with the US was

[low]{.mark}er-[profile]{.mark}, non-threatening foreign [policy
is]{.mark} the [more likely]{.mark} Chinese [response to]{.mark}
perceived self-[decline]{.mark}.]{.underline}

While making their case that Beijing feels pressured to rush into war
this decade, Hal Brands and Michael Beckley observe that "[[other

gradually joining forces [to [check Beijing's power]{.mark} and put it
in a strategic box." This observation]{.underline} is correct, but it
actually [undermines their larger argument]{.underline}. The pushback,
which includes consolidation of the Quad joint security alliance
([[Australia]{.mark}, Japan, US and [India]{.mark}) [and]{.mark} the
increased likelihood that [Japan would]{.mark} help [defend Taiwan,
is]{.mark} already [so formidable]{.mark} as [to]{.mark} probably [deter
China from embarking on]{.mark} a campaign of military
[aggression]{.mark} even in the 2020s]{.underline}. 

#### China can't overtake the US. 

**Pei 21**, Professor of Government at Claremont McKenna College,
Bloomberg Opinion Columnist (Minxin Pei, May 27, 2021, "The China Threat
Is Being Overhyped," Bloomberg,
https://www.bloomberg.com/opinion/articles/2021-05-27/u-s-shouldn-t-treat-china-as-an-existential-threat)

The fact is, those throwing around the charge most liberally don't seem
to have thought through what it would really mean for China to threaten
the existence of the world's most powerful nation. In reality, [[China
has neither]{.mark} the destructive [capability nor]{.mark} the
geopolitical [motivation]{.mark} to destroy the U.S.]{.underline}

Most obviously, unlike the former Soviet Union, China does not possess a
nuclear arsenal large and powerful enough to obliterate the U.S. And,
[[while]{.mark} its [conventional power]{.mark} has
[grown]{.mark}]{.underline} to the point where it could inflict [serious
losses](https://www.yahoo.com/lifestyle/were-going-to-lose-fast-us-air-force-held-a-war-game-that-started-with-a-chinese-biological-attack-170003936.html) on
U.S. forces [[in]{.mark} a [conflict over Taiwan, China]{.mark}'s
military significantly [lags the U.S. in tech]{.mark}nological
sophistication [and warfighting experience]{.mark}]{.underline}. In any
wider clash, [the [U.S. would]{.mark} also be able to [count on]{.mark}
the support of its [allies]{.mark} and China's regional rivals,
including [Japan and India]{.mark}]{.underline}, which themselves have
potent militaries. [[China]{.mark} will [be alone and face enemies on
many sides]{.mark}.]{.underline}

As for motivation, China is deeply resentful of the American security
presence in Asia. Its leaders see the U.S. as the principal obstacle to
their country's global ambitions. But there is no evidence that they are
bent on regime change in the U.S. or seek its physical destruction.

[[China has no]{.mark} real ideological [appeal]{.mark} outside its
borders]{.underline}, despite assiduously promoting its "[ China
Model](https://www.bloomberg.com/quicktake/the-china-model?sref=BthlAIW9)"
of rapid economic development under one-party rule; [[most]{.mark} other
[countries know]{.mark} that [China's]{.mark} economic [success]{.mark}
would [be hard to replicate]{.mark}]{.underline}. During the height of
the Cold War in the 1950s and 1960s, Soviet communism inspired
revolutions around the world. Today, [the few countries that pay lip
service to the China Model do so to win economic favors from Beijing.
The real ideological threat to the U.S.]{.underline} --- the political
[authoritarianism embodied by]{.underline} former President Donald
[Trump]{.underline} and his followers --- [lies within its own
borders.]{.underline}

What about the prospect of China becoming the world's largest economy in
dollar terms? Measured in real terms (or purchasing power parity), the
Chinese
economy [surpassed](https://www.wsj.com/articles/BL-REB-24899) America's
in 2014 without eroding U.S. leadership in global finance and
technology, or undermining U.S. prosperity.

Judging the Chinese threat by the size of China's economy (the amount of
goods and services it produces) is also misleading because its
population is more than four times larger than that of the U.S.
Aggregate wealth and technological capabilities are far more meaningful
yardsticks of power.

Compared with China, the U.S. remains a solid leader in both. U.S.
households held [\$130
trillion](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-usa-fed-wealth/u-s-households-ended-2020-with-record-130-2-trillion-in-wealth-fed-says-idUSKBN2B32H5) in
wealth at the end of 2020, while Credit Suisse estimates that Chinese
household wealth in mid-2020 stood at \$82 trillion.

[[Technologically]{.underline}]{.mark}, while China has made impressive
strides in recent years, [the [U.S. continues to lead]{.mark} in key
areas. The recent tech [trade war]{.mark}]{.underline} between the two
countries [[exposed]{.mark} just [how vulnerable
China]{.mark}]{.underline} --- not the U.S. --- [[is]{.mark}. U.S.
sanctions have crippled China's telecom giant Huawei]{.underline}
Technologies Co. and cast a shadow over the entire Chinese tech sector.
[Even China's]{.underline} impressive recent [Mars landing depended on
technology comparable to what NASA was using in 2003.]{.underline}

None of this even takes into account [[China's]{.mark} manifold
[internal weaknesses]{.mark}]{.underline}, which [range
from]{.underline} rapid [[demographic deterioration]{.mark} to [social
unrest]{.mark}, ethnic [tensions and]{.mark} an inefficient
[state-capitalist]{.mark} system. All [will limit]{.mark} the
[growth]{.mark} of Chinese power.]{.underline}

#### China is structurally constrained\-\--they speak loudly without carrying a big stick. 

**Weiss 23**, Professor of Government, Cornell University, Senior Fellow
at the Asia Society Policy Institute's Center for China Analysis
(Jessica Chen Weiss, May 4, 2023, "Even China Isn't Convinced It Can
Replace the U.S.," New York Times,
https://www.nytimes.com/2023/05/04/opinion/china-us-world-order.html)

Leading [[Chinese]{.underline}]{.mark} intellectuals [openly
[acknowledge]{.mark} the [difficulty]{.mark} of [reconciling what China
says with what it does]{.mark}. "Even we don't believe much of what we
say,"]{.underline} the Chinese economist Yao Yang, who is known for his
pragmatic views, has said. "Our goal is not to defeat liberalism, but
instead to say that what we have can be as good as what you have." Jiang
Shigong, a legal scholar and apologist for Mr. Xi's political
philosophy, has written that "'socialism' is not ossified dogma, but
instead an open concept awaiting exploration and definition."

China's long-term ambitions are difficult to know with certainty, and
they can change. But [[it is far from clear]{.mark} that [it
can]{.mark}]{.underline} --- or even seeks to --- [[replace]{.mark} the
[U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [as the]{.mark} world's [dominant
power]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Mr. Xi and the C.C.P. apparently see the United States as trying to keep
China perpetually subordinate and vulnerable, opposing whatever China
does or advocates in an international system that Beijing believes
favors the United States and developed democracies. But at a minimum,
China seems more intent on modifying aspects of a system under which it
has prospered --- making it safer for autocracy --- rather than
replacing it.

Mr. Xi often couches this effort in his political slogans like the
"China dream" and a "shared future for humankind." But there is
continuing debate in China over what these visions really mean and what
costs and risks China should accept in seeking global leadership.
China's overseas development largess, for example, is limited by the
imperative of addressing its own persistent development needs at home,
research by the scholar Min Ye has shown. Same for other key Chinese
strategies for widening its influence: Its efforts to internationalize
the renminbi and reduce dollar dominance are constrained by the tight
grip it keeps on the currency's value, as well as other capital
controls. These policies help stabilize its economy and prevent capital
flight, but they limit the renminbi's global appeal.

U.S. concerns often center on the legitimate fear that China could
attack Taiwan. But despite menacing Chinese military exercises meant to
deter the self-ruled island from moving closer to formal independence,
many experts believe that Beijing still prefers to achieve its
longstanding objective of "peaceful reunification" through measures
short of war. China could lose in a war and face international sanctions
and supply chain disruptions. These would be economically and
politically devastating, jeopardizing Mr. Xi's prime objectives of
regime security, domestic stability and national rejuvenation.

[Doubts are growing that China, [facing economic headwinds and]{.mark} a
[shrinking population]{.mark}, can achieve its goal of surpassing the
United States]{.underline} as the world's largest economy, let alone
other metrics of global leadership. There is broad recognition in
[China]{.underline} that it [[remains militarily]{.mark}, economically
[and technologically weaker]{.mark} than the United States]{.underline}
and that further modernization depends upon continued access to
international technology, capital and markets within a stable economic
order. "It is impossible for America to contain the rise of China," the
influential Chinese scholar Huang Renwei has noted, "and it is equally
impossible for China to quickly surpass America."

Chinese rhetoric about global governance reform has resonated in many
developing countries that also see international institutions as tilted
against them. But [there is [little reason]{.mark} to believe that the
[C.C.P.]{.mark}'s]{.underline} self-serving, [[nationalist ideology will
captivate the world]{.mark}, especially [as]{.mark} Mr. [Xi feeds
mistrust with]{.mark} his [authoritarian]{.mark} ways, coercive [tactics
against]{.mark} foreign [businesses and trading
partners]{.mark}]{.underline}, and policies that increasingly smack of
paranoia. China tends to be viewed more favorably in parts of the
developing world. But that owes more to economics than to ideas, and its
overseas [[investments]{.mark} have often [been criticized for]{.mark}
lacking [transparency, saddling]{.mark} poor [countries with
debt]{.mark}, as well as environmental and other concerns.]{.underline}

#### China's "dream" is gone\-\--too economically weak. 

**Linden 23**, Professor Emeritus of Political Science at the University
of Pittsburgh (Robert Linden, April 3, 2023, "Is the Chinese Dream
Turning into a Chinese Nightmare for Beijing?" National Interest,
https://nationalinterest.org/feature/chinese-dream-turning-chinese-nightmare-beijing-206374?page=0%2C1)

Far from being astride the globe, [[the "China Dream"]{.mark}
globally---China's economic power, political attraction, and
standing---[are]{.mark} all [eroding]{.mark}]{.underline}. Several key
indicators reveal that, in the epic clash with the United States and the
"collective West," [China is weaker than at any time]{.underline} in the
last ten years.

Consider the economic dimensions for starters. A key instrument of great
power influence has long been foreign direct investment (FDI), something
that is also crucial for China's own economic health. Spurred by its
2001 "Go Out" policy and supercharged [[with]{.mark} the]{.underline}
Belt and Road Initiative [([BRI]{.mark}) [in 2013,
Chinese]{.mark}]{.underline} outward [[FDI grew]{.mark}
steadily]{.underline}, from \$10 billion in 2005 to more than \$170
billion by 2017. [[Since]{.mark} then]{.underline}, four of [the
[last]{.mark}]{.underline} five [[years]{.mark} have [seen drops
in]{.mark} outward [FDI]{.mark}---including a 15 percent fall in
2022]{.underline}, according to the American Enterprise Institute.
[[Chinese investment]{.underline}]{.mark} into Europe, previously a
choice location because of its high value-added manufacturing capacity
and weak regulation, [[fell sharply]{.mark} as well. Once eager
[partners]{.mark}]{.underline} like Germany, Italy, and the EU itself
[have [adopted]{.mark}]{.underline} or strengthened [investment
[screening]{.mark}]{.underline} mechanisms [[and blocked]{.mark} key
Chinese [acquisitions]{.mark}]{.underline}. A mutual investment treaty
that is supposed to address chronic business complaints about Chinese
practices and restrictions has stalled in the European Parliament due to
widespread Chinese human rights violations and tit-for-tat individual
sanctions. Viewed in the other direction, European investment into China
dropped steadily after 2018 until rebounding last year. But as [Rhodium
Group figures show](https://rhg.com/research/the-chosen-few/), FDI
inflow to China has become concentrated to the point where almost 90
percent of European FDI in China comes from only four countries. During
the coronavirus pandemic, the Rhodium report notes, "virtually no
European investors that were not already present in the country have
made direct investments." The impact of European investment, which had
in 2018 accounted for 7.5 percent of Chinese GDP, fell to 2.8 percent
three years later.

The most serious challenge to Chinese economic clout has come from the
United States. [Heightened [tariffs]{.mark} and
restrictions]{.underline} put in place during the Trump administration
[have [continued under]{.mark} Joe [Biden]{.mark}]{.underline}. Reviews
of foreign (read: Chinese) investments have broadened, as have policies
[[blocking]{.mark} the sale of high-[tech goods to
China]{.mark}]{.underline}---not only from the United States, but also
from companies in other countries whose goods have U.S. components.
Washington redoubled its efforts to block Huawei and TikTok, along with
passing legislation to subsidize U.S. production of high-tech goods at
home and direct "friendshoring" investments to reliable allies and
partners.

As an economic colossus, China has alternatives, especially [[in Asia,
Africa]{.mark}, and other places]{.underline} where it claims to offer
an alternative development model. But here, too, [[China's
presence]{.mark} has [run out of steam]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[Annual [investments]{.mark}]{.underline} in the countries of the BRI,
once the flagship vehicle for the extension of Chinese influence,
[[are]{.mark} today [less than half of]{.mark} what they were only [five
years ago]{.mark}]{.underline}. And [[most]{.mark} of that is [in
countries with]{.mark} serious [debt]{.mark} issues]{.underline}. As a
report in Foreign Policy put it, "China can make friends or break legs.
It can't do both."

#### Yes impact\-\--demographic and economic trends will imperil Chinese military ambitions. 

**Stokes 23**, Visiting Fellow, German Marshall Fund, Washington DC
(Bruce Stokes, January 22, 2023, "China may prove not to be the threat

#### Structural constraints prevent both force projection and soft power influence\-\--containment not required. 

**Mueller 22**, Political Scientist, Ohio State University, Senior
Fellow, CATO Institute (John Mueller, January 11, 2022, "China Has Been
a Failure at Hegemony, So Let's Just Chill," CATO Institute,
https://www.cato.org/commentary/china-has-been-failure-hegemony-so-lets-just-chill)

[[Many]{.underline}]{.mark} in the U.S. foreign policy establishment and
elsewhere [[are sounding]{.mark} an [alarm over]{.mark}]{.underline}
concerns that, as [[China]{.underline}]{.mark} develops, it
will [become](https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/china/2021-10-19/inevitable-rivalry-cold-war) the
dominant power in its region, [a ["hegemon"]{.mark} that
will]{.underline} have too much "influence" there and do [damage to U.S.
security interests]{.underline}. For example, in 2017 the National
Intelligence
Council [opined](https://www.dni.gov/files/documents/nic/GT-Full-Report.pdf) that
"geopolitical competition" was on the rise and the Chinese sought "to
exert more sway over their neighboring regions and promote an order in
which U.S. influence does not dominate."

Accordingly, as it is
often [suggested](https://www.amazon.com/Strategy-Denial-American-Defense-Conflict/dp/0300256434),
military hardware must be deployed to somehow keep that from happening.

[[However]{.underline}]{.mark}, to a degree, [[we already know what
Chinese "hegemony" looks like]{.underline}]{.mark}. Over the last
decade, China has established a major military and especially economic
presence, and it has tried to convert this condition into influence.
These experiments in hegemony --- China's Belt and Road Initiative (BRI)
which has stressed construction loans to countries across Eurasia, and
its rather belligerent "wolf diplomacy" antics --- have substantially
foundered and have, if anything, proved to be counterproductive. The
experience does not bode well for future efforts, and it does not
justify alarm.

Its much‐​hyped [[B]{.mark}elt and [R]{.mark}oad
[I]{.mark}nitiative]{.underline}
was [once](https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/china-has-a-plan-to-rule-the-world/2017/11/28/214299aa-d472-11e7-a986-d0a9770d9a3e_story.html) [deemed](https://www.amazon.com/Battlegrounds-Fight-Defend-Free-World/dp/0062899465) by
some to be a key element of a plot by the Chinese to "rule the world."
However, from the beginning there
were [warnings](https://www.cfr.org/book/third-revolution) from Chinese
scholars and business leaders questioning the economic rationale for
many of the investments. In addition, economist Barry Naughton of the
University of California at San
Diego [notes](https://www.amazon.com/Fateful-Decisions-Shorenstein-Asia-Pacific-Research-ebook/dp/B086ZXCBNY) that
the idea was in part economically misguided, and Tufts University's
Michael Beckley
has [estimated](https://www.amazon.com/Unrivaled-America-Superpower-Cornell-Security/dp/1501724789) that
the scheme would "probably exacerbate China's woes" by funding "hundreds
of financially dubious projects in unstable countries, more than half of
which have credit ratings below investment‐​grade."

These suggestions have proved to be justified. Expenditures of hundreds
of billions on the project [have [failed to deliver]{.mark} either
[returns]{.mark} for investors]{.underline} (including state‐​run banks)
[or political returns for
China]{.underline}, [notes](https://foreignpolicy.com/2018/12/06/bri-china-belt-road-initiative-blunder/?utm_source=PostUp&utm_medium=email&utm_campaign=China%20Brief%20OC&utm_term=37767&tpcc=China%20Brief%20OC) Taiwan‐​based
analyst Tanner Green. [The project was there]{.underline}, he says,
"[only because it is the favored brainchild of an]{.underline}
authoritarian leader living in an [echo chamber]{.underline}" --- for
other Chinese to attack BRI is "to attack the legitimacy of the party
itself."

By 2019, [BRI [lending]{.mark}]{.underline} by China
[had [[fallen](https://www.ft.com/content/d9bd8059-d05c-4e6f-968b-1672241ec1f6) from]{.mark}
a peak of [\$75 billion]{.mark} in 2016]{.underline} (at a time when its
promulgating author, Chinese President Xi Jinping, was touting BRI as "a
project of the century") [[to just \$4]{.mark} billion]{.underline}.
Although some of these projects will likely succeed, there were reports
by the end of 2020, like this one by James Kynge and Jonathan Wheatley
at the Financial Times, that the money had often been doled out "with
a combination of hubris, ambition, and naivete." Descriptors like
"unravelling," "fallen off a cliff," and "ill‐​conceived" were being
applied by experts, including Matt Ferchen at Merics, a Berlin‐​based
think‐​tank, who wrote that China was now "mired in debt renegotiations
with a host of countries." 

In fact, as Stanford's Elizabeth Economy notes, there has been something
of a backlash and "stories of [Chinese [corruption and scandals]{.mark}
with infrastructure projects [are contributing]{.mark}]{.underline} to
rising Sinophobia." China has shot
back [saying](https://www.cnbc.com/2019/03/30/critics-of-chinas-belt-and-road-initiative-are-prejudiced-diplomat.html) critics
are "prejudiced" and lacking "objectivity and a fair understanding" of
the initiative; others have sought to play down criticisms of
Chinese labor practices or accusations of [debt
traps](https://www.chathamhouse.org/2020/08/debunking-myth-debt-trap-diplomacy). 

Rising concerns have nonetheless pushed the European Union to launch
a global investment project that is apparently intended to be a counter
to China's BRI, and the United States seems to want to
follow [suit](https://foreignpolicy.com/2021/06/29/biden-build-back-better-world-belt-road-initiative/).
But, as Foreign Policy's James Palmer noted recently, "all this feels
both outdated and unnecessary," pointing out that "the heyday of BRI
hype, even in China, was at least three years ago," while the current
focus is on the "white elephant nature of projects and the relative lack
of deliverables for China."

China's clumsy "wolf warrior" diplomacy of recent years has also failed
to deliver. As Stanford's Thomas
Fingar [notes](https://www.amazon.com/Fateful-Decisions-Shorenstein-Asia-Pacific-Research-ebook/dp/B086ZXCBNY),
"muscular displays of [Chinese [military power]{.mark} may have [been
intended to dissuade]{.mark} neighboring [countries from lending support
to]{.mark} imputed [U.S.]{.mark} military planning]{.underline}, but
they seriously undercut efforts to reassure other countries that they
had no reason to fear China's 'peaceful rise.'"

Moreover, [they [have pushed]{.mark} countries]{.underline} it sought to
intimidate, [such as [Japan, India, So]{.mark}uth [Ko]{.mark}rea, [and
Australia, to become]{.mark} far [more hostile]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Thus, historian Arne Westad [points
out](https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/china/2019-08-12/sources-chinese-conduct) that
the efforts "have all backfired: East Asia is much warier of Chinese
aims today than it was a decade ago," and he cites a [Pew Research
Center
poll](https://www.pewresearch.org/global/2020/10/06/unfavorable-views-of-china-reach-historic-highs-in-many-countries/pg_2020-10-06_global-views-china_0-01/) showing
the percentage of South Koreans who viewed China's rise favorably fell
from 66 percent in 2002 to 34 percent in 2017. In Australia the
percentage trusting China to act responsibly in the
world [dropped](https://poll.lowyinstitute.org/themes/china/) from 52
percent to 16 percent between 2018 and 2021.

As David Shambaugh, a China specialist at George Washington
University, [concludes](https://oxford.universitypressscholarship.com/view/10.1093/oso/9780190062316.001.0001/oso-9780190062316-chapter-16):
"If Beijing is trying to recreate a twenty‐​first‐​century version of the
imperial 'tribute system,' it will inevitably fail, as [other sovereign
[Asian nations do not desire]{.mark} to fall into [such]{.mark}
a]{.underline} patron‐​client [[relationship with
China]{.underline}]{.mark} again."

Fingar and Jean
Oi [summarize](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/0163660X.2020.1734304) the
situation: "China's relationship with more or less all countries is more
fraught today than it was before Xi launched the BRI and China began to
flex its economic and military muscles in ways neighbors found
worrisome."

More broadly, these experiences suggest (as was seen [as
well](https://www.amazon.com/Stupidity-War-American-Foreign-Complacency/dp/1108843832) in
the Cold War) that [[containment is scarcely
required]{.underline}]{.mark} because political "dominance" (such as it
is) does not flow naturally from economic or military growth.
