### AT: Grid !\-\--1NC

#### Grid damage is reversible, and [segmentation]{.underline} prevents cascades

**Lambert 23**, citing **Daniel Brooks**, MS, vice president of
integrated grid and energy systems at EPRI, a nonprofit research and
development institute. (Hannah Ray, 1-16-2023, \"What is the national
power grid and how resilient is it?\", Fox News,
https://www.foxnews.com/us/national-power-grid-resilient)

Historic weather events, cyberattacks and even physical assaults on
power stations have raised concerns about the vulnerability of the
nation\'s electric grid.

But what exactly is the grid and how resilient is it against threats of
all kinds?

[The grid is made up of power generators]{.underline} --- such as dams,
coal plants or wind turbines --- [and more than five million miles of
power lines]{.underline} carrying electricity all over the country,
according to the vice president of integrated grid and energy systems at
EPRI, a nonprofit research and development institute.

\"All of that is working together to make sure that when you flip that
switch on in your house, the light comes on just then,\" Daniel Brooks
said. \"So it\'s pretty complicated.\"

[Overall, [the grid is \"extremely reliable,\"]{.mark}]{.underline} he
said. But with more of the U.S. electrified than at any other point in
history, the grid also faces more threats.

\"We\'ve seen an increasing number of what folks would call a
one-in-100-year type of event,\" Brooks told Fox News.

Extreme weather events like record-breaking wildfires in California,
hurricanes and powerful winter storms have become larger considerations
for grid planners, Brooks said. But weather is not the only concern.

WHAT IS IT? THE NATIONAL POWER GRID:

\"There are bad guys out there that would like to be able to leverage
vulnerabilities that they could find with cyber or maybe physical
security,\" Brooks said.

Gunfire erupted this week at a substation in Randolph County, North
Carolina. While it did not cause any outages, it comes less than two
months after more than 40,000 electric customers in North Carolina were
left in the dark after someone shot at two substations.

On Christmas morning, vandals struck four substations in Washington,
knocking out power to about 15,000 customers. Pundits and activists soon
speculated whether the increase in attacks on power stations could be
the work of \"right-wing extremists.\"

The motive in both North Carolina cases remains unknown and no one has
been arrested. But the men now charged with attacking the substations in
Washington allegedly planned to commit a burglary while the power was
out.

\"Those aren\'t the normal events that we actually design and operate
the grid through,\" Brooks said.

But [grid planners]{.underline} \"[really want to avoid any]{.underline}
type of [event that could result in a long duration
outage]{.underline},\" which could threaten peoples\' health and safety,
he added.

[Transmission [towers have become]{.mark} increasingly [hardened against
winds]{.mark}]{.underline}, he said. [Some power [lines are designed
so]{.mark} that if a tree]{.underline} limb [falls across them, the
[lines drop away]{.mark} from the pole rather [than dragging down the
entire pole]{.mark}]{.underline} which would take much longer to fix.

\"[Part of resiliency is making sure]{.underline} that [[we don\'t
get]{.underline}]{.mark} those [[long duration outages by putting in
systems that allow us to]{.underline}]{.mark} be able to [[recover
quickly]{.underline}]{.mark},\" he said.

National power grid three regional interconnections

[[The]{.mark} national]{.underline} power [[grid is]{.mark} actually
[made up of three]{.mark}]{.underline} loosely-connected
[[regions]{.underline}]{.mark}. [That\'s good news when it comes to
resiliency because [it means if there\'s a]{.mark} serious [problem in
the West]{.mark}]{.underline}ern interconnection, [[the
East]{.underline}]{.mark}ern U.S. [[should be fine]{.underline}]{.mark},
Brooks said.

\"[[An issue that]{.mark} starts to happen]{.underline} and hopefully
[[would never]{.mark} actually [cascade into a]{.mark} complete
[blackout]{.mark}, [that wouldn\'t go beyond those individual
grids]{.mark},\"]{.underline} he said.

A diverse array of power sources is also key to grid resiliency, Brooks
said. Solar power might perform well during the day, but the benefits
vanish when the sun does, so other resources such as nuclear, gas or
coal might pick up the slack.

### AT: Grid !\-\--2NC

#### No blackouts.

**Koerth 18**, Senior Science Writer for FiveThirtyEight, citing Bill
Lawrence, a Vice President and Chief Security Officer at the North
American Electric Reliability Corporation (Maggie Koerth, August 13,
2018, "Hacking The Electric Grid Is Damned Hard," 538,
https://fivethirtyeight.com/features/hacking-the-electric-grid-is-damned-hard/)

Representatives from two nonprofit organizations --- both of which play
large roles in how the electric grid is regulated and maintained ---
said it is easier to imagine disaster scenarios than create one.
"There've been some very sensational books out there about the grid
going dark because someone's got their finger ready over a mouse and
everything is going to turn off at the same time," said Bill Lawrence,
vice president and chief security officer at the North American Electric
Reliability Corporation, the regulatory authority that sets and enforces
technological standards for utility companies across the continent. "The
grid does not work that way." [[Our]{.mark} electric
[infra]{.mark}structure [is chock-full of]{.mark}]{.underline} both
[[redundancies and]{.mark} regional [variations]{.mark} --- two things
[that impede widespread sabotage]{.mark}.]{.underline}

That's not to say that the grid isn't under attack. Lawrence
acknowledged that there is interest in "trying to hurt us from a
distance." But he emphasized there have not yet been
any successful attacks --- meaning hackers haven't caused any blackouts.

They've been poking at our critical infrastructure for a long while.
Incident reports published by the Industrial Control Systems Cyber
Emergency Response Team --- a division of Homeland Security that does
training and responds to cyberattacks on critical infrastructure ---
suggest that electricity, oil and natural gas infrastructure have been
routinely targeted for years.1 There are dozens of these attacks
reported to ICS-CERTS annually.

However, [it would be difficult]{.underline} for these attacks [to lead
to wide-scale blackouts]{.underline}, according to Lawrence and Candace
Suh-Lee, who leads a cybersecurity research team at the Electric Power
Research Institute, a nonprofit research and development lab. And that's
true even if hackers do eventually succeed in taking control of some
electric systems.

It helps that [[the]{.mark} North [American]{.mark} electric [grid
is]{.mark} both [diverse in]{.mark} its [engineering and
redundant]{.mark} in its design]{.underline}. For instance, the
Ukrainian attacks are often cited as evidence that hundreds of thousands
of Americans could suddenly find themselves in the dark because of
hackers. But Lawrence considers the Ukrainian grid a lot easier to
infiltrate than the North American one. That's because Ukraine's
infrastructure is more homogeneous, the result of electrification
happening under the standardizing eye of the former Soviet Union, he
told me. [The North American grid]{.underline}, in contrast, [began as
[a patchwork of unconnected]{.mark} electric [islands]{.mark}, each
[designed]{.mark} and built [by companies]{.mark} that [weren't
coordinating]{.mark} with one another]{.underline}. Even today, he said,
[the enforceable [standards]{.mark}]{.underline} set by NERC [[don't
tell you]{.mark} exactly [what to buy]{.mark}]{.underline} or how to
build. "So [[taking down one]{.mark} utility [and going]{.mark} right
[next door and doing the same]{.mark} thing]{.underline} to that
neighboring utility [[would be]{.mark} an extremely [difficult]{.mark}
challenge]{.underline}," he said.

Meanwhile, [[the]{.mark} electric [grid]{.mark} already
[contains]{.mark} a lot of [redundancies]{.mark}]{.underline} that are
built in to prevent blackouts caused by common problems like broken tree
limbs or heat waves --- and those redundancies would also help to
prevent a successful cyberattack from affecting a large number of
people. Suh-Lee pointed to an August 2003 blackout that turned the
lights off on 50 million people on the east coast of the U.S. and
Canada. "When we analyzed it, there was about 17 different things lined
up that went wrong. Then it happened," she said. Hackers wouldn't
necessarily have control over all the things that would have to go wrong
to create a blackout like that.

### AT: China War !\-\--1NC

#### No China war or adventurism. 

**Nolt 24**, PhD, Adjunct Professor at New York University, Senior
Fellow at the World Policy Institute. (James H., 1-29-2024, \"Geography
Limits China's Possibilities as a Sea Power,\" China-US Focus,
https://www.chinausfocus.com/peace-security/geography-limits-chinas-possibilities-as-a-sea-power)

Despite some easing of U.S.-China tensions, [there remains a steady
drumbeat of pundit]{.underline} and politician [warnings about the
supposed dire military threat from China]{.underline}. [I have been
publishing articles on the military balance [in]{.mark} East
[Asia]{.mark} for nearly three decades]{.underline}, each one refuting
the notion that the threat of Chinese aggression is imminent; [[peace
prevails]{.underline}]{.mark}, as I predicted.

[[The reasons are]{.mark} several: [nuclear
deterrence]{.mark}]{.underline}, [the [vastly greater]{.mark} [military
spending of the U.S.]{.mark}]{.underline} and its allies, [[a balance of
forces that favors US heg]{.underline}]{.mark}emony in the air and on
the sea, the [[lack of]{.mark} serious [Chinese intent to
launch]{.mark}]{.underline} a career of
[[aggression]{.underline}]{.mark}, [which is
[illustrated]{.mark}]{.underline} more [[by]{.underline}]{.mark}
actions---[[a]{.underline}]{.mark} steady and fairly [[low
percent]{.underline}]{.mark}age [[of]{.underline}]{.mark} its [[GDP
spent on military]{.mark} preparations]{.underline}---than by sometimes
muscular rhetoric, etc. However, [there is one major consideration
seldom acknowledged that makes China's capacity to project
power]{.underline} forcefully [far weaker]{.underline}
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} less sensitive to marginal changes in the
balance of power that is largely ignored:
[[geography]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Like Russia, Germany and the former Austria-Hungary, [China is largely
landlocked]{.underline}. No matter how big a navy Germany tried to
build, Britain stood as an unassailable fortress astride its sea lines
of communication, easily able to intercept its warships and interdict
its trade. Even during World War Two, when Germany gained the ports of
France, its ability to operate submarines across the Atlantic improved,
but its trade outside the Baltic remained severed. Most of its large
warships were lost during futile attempts to operate past Britain. The
USSR was similarly constrained geographically by the US and its many
allies during the Cold War. Its fleet was divided into four
geographically isolated parts, none of which had free access to open
ocean without passing dangerously near U.S. allies and bases. Overseas
trade for the USSR during wartime would have been hopeless. Even against
a much smaller power, Japan, during their 1904-05 war, Russia could not
concentrate its much larger fleet. Its Pacific bases were easily
blockaded by Japanese naval forces. These powers were all constrained
from being sea powers, not just by the unfavorable balance of forces,
but even more by geographical barriers.

Military propagandists largely neglect these geographical realities. It
is long been said that China is building a "blue water navy" capable of
traversing the world's oceans as a great power. Yes, but only in peace
time. [[The day]{.mark} that [any]{.mark} major [war breaks out, China's
overseas trade stops]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[Its
ports]{.underline}]{.mark}, [but not those of its
adversaries]{.underline}, [[are instantly
blockaded]{.underline}]{.mark}. Any warships outside its home waters
would be tracked down and sunk. This geographical fact is insensitive to
the balance of forces. [China's navy could be five times as big; it
still would not]{.underline} [be a]{.underline} true [blue water navy
because during a]{.underline} shooting [war its fleet would be
vulnerable even in its own]{.underline} coastal waters, yet also unable
to venture into the open seas without catastrophic losses. This is
similar to Germany's situation in both world wars.

There is a very important reason [geography is even more constraining
today]{.underline} than it would have been a century ago: [air power
trumps sea power]{.underline}. World War Two proved that surface navies
cannot operate safely outside the cover of friendly air power. Only
three navies then, the American, British, and Japanese, operated enough
aircraft carriers to venture near enemy land-based air control. During
1943, with irreplaceable losses of aircraft carriers and veteran pilots,
plus the American completion of large classes of new carriers, the
far-flung Japanese Empire lost the capacity to defend its own internal
shipping lanes, island bases, and surface navy. Its defeat became
inevitable.

[The lessons of naval combat since World War Two have only further
amplified air power dominance]{.underline}. New forms of land-based air
power, including drones and anti-shipping missiles (ASMs), are ever more
cost-effective additions to the arsenals of land-powers geographically
able to interdict sea trade and naval operations. Most pundits have
missed this as well, or even gotten it backwards.

Most recently, [the Houthi attacks on shipping]{.underline} through the
vital Red Sea have [been used to exaggerate the potential Chinese threat
to shipping]{.underline} in the event of a war in East Asia. Once again,
[geography is ignored]{.underline}. [The Houthi forces are astride a
very narrow part of the shortest route from Europe to Asia]{.underline}.
Shipping can divert around Africa, but the route is much longer and thus
more expensive. [China does have ASMs and drones]{.underline} in
abundance, [but most can only reach its own coastal waters]{.underline},
[which are critical for its own shipping]{.underline}---and
Taiwan's[---but not anybody else's]{.underline}. [Ships from Japan or
So]{.underline}uth [Ko]{.underline}rea [that now pass through the
E]{.underline}ast [C]{.underline}hina [S]{.underline}ea [could easily
divert east of the Philippines]{.underline}, out of range of air power
based in China. China's geographical situation lacks the Houthi
advantage.

[Even Chinese sub]{.underline}marine[s]{.underline} [would be vulnerable
to air power]{.underline} outside the protection of their land-based
fighter aircraft, which reach only about 500 kilometers from its land
bases. [[China's few]{.mark} aircraft [carriers]{.mark}]{.underline}
with small fighter forces embarked [[would have a hard time defending
themselves]{.mark} from land-based air attacks]{.underline}---let alone
from any of the larger and far more numerous U.S. aircraft carriers---to
be of much use, except perhaps against an especially weak regional
power.

[The effectiveness of the Houthi]{.underline}---[and]{.underline} also
of [Ukraine]{.underline} [against the Russian Black Sea
fleet]{.underline}---[underlines China's]{.underline} own greater
[vulnerability to the new generation of naval weapons]{.underline},
drones and ASMs, in addition to conventional air power. [Chinese
[warships]{.mark}]{.underline}, not to mention transports carrying
troops for any seaborne invasion, such as against Taiwan, [[are
vulnerable even within its own]{.mark} coastal
[waters]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Such aerial weapons are relatively
inexpensive]{.underline}, highly [accurate]{.underline}, easy to hide,
[and not]{.underline} so [easy to intercept]{.underline}. This was
demonstrated by [[the handful of Ukrainian missiles]{.underline}]{.mark}
that [[destroyed]{.mark} most]{.underline} of the major [[Russian
warships]{.underline}]{.mark} in the Black Sea, which were defended as
well as Chinese ships would be. Even a power controlling the air has
considerable difficulty finding and suppressing ASMs and drones before
they can be launched. Taiwan has a vastly larger arsenal of ASMs than
does Ukraine. Furthermore, in the event of a crisis in the Taiwan
Straits, the U.S. could rapidly airlift additional supplies to Taiwan,
largely neutralizing any Chinese invasion threat. In any case, [[China's
amphibious lift]{.underline}]{.mark} [capability [is]{.mark} [too
small]{.mark} to transport and supply a force anywhere near large enough
to overcome Taiwan's ground forces]{.underline}. Effectively, China is
constrained by geography. Given this, the balance of forces and
capabilities is not sensitive to incremental developments.

### AT: China War !\-\--2NC

#### No China war. 

**von Sydow 24**, a China analyst at Swedish National China Centre.
(Alexis, 2-23-2024, "Most Experts Agree: China Isn't About to Invade
Taiwan," The Diplomat,
https://thediplomat.com/2024/02/most-experts-agree-china-isnt-about-to-invade-taiwan/)

[[Alarmist]{.mark} commentators [overestimate]{.mark} the [risk of
war]{.mark} in]{.underline} at least [three important ways]{.underline}.

First, it is often stated that China is arming itself specifically in
preparation for a Taiwan invasion. 2027 often figures as a possible date
for such an invasion, presumably because this year is an official
milestone in the Chinese military's modernization plans. But the year
2027 hardly figures at all in the scholarly forecasts we have examined. 

Concerning China's military modernization, forecasts do point to growing
risk, but this is mainly a question of the gradually changing military
balance vis-a-vis the United States in the region. [A more even military
balance]{.underline} inherently means growing risk, but that risk [is
growing from a very low baseline probability. It is still true that
[China cannot be confident to win a war]{.mark} with the U.S. over
Taiwan, which keeps down the risk]{.underline} for the present.

Second, it is said that China cannot accept Taiwan's democratic
institutions, and that conflict can only be averted by the United States
and other allies stepping up explicit support for Taiwan's independence.
But forecasts instead point to the worsening China-U.S. relationship
itself as one of the major risk factors. Growing tensions over Taiwan
are a consequence of increasing great power conflict. [[Beijing sees
Taiwan as]{.mark} a [part of]{.mark} the [U.S.
s]{.mark}]{.underline}phere [[o]{.underline}]{.mark}f
[[i]{.underline}]{.mark}nfluence, [[and knows]{.mark} that an
[attack]{.mark} on Taiwan [is a]{.mark} military [attack on U.S.]{.mark}
interests]{.underline}. If [China-U.S. [rivalry is kept within limits,
China has little interest in provoking]{.mark} such a
[conflict]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Third, a common narrative among alarmists is the story of the changing
Chinese regime. They warn us of China's increasingly aggressive posture,
its growing "assertiveness," and an alleged ideological turn away from
"openness" under the rule of Xi Jinping. By contrast, forecasters are
divided on the question of whether China's intentions concerning Taiwan
have changed in any crucial regard. Xi Jinping hews close to the
traditional Communist Party line of unquestioned sovereignty over
Taiwan, and his international ambitions are likewise inherited from his
predecessors. Most of China's new-found "assertiveness" is likely less a
matter of ideological change and more a matter of growing capabilities.
From this, one might conclude that assumptions of extreme urgency on
China's part are faulty.  

These are the takeaways from available forecasts, and they stand in
stark contrast to alarmist predictions from the U.S. military and other
actors. They suggest that alarmist narratives are arrived at by looking
at the growing risk of war in isolation, without consideration of its
general low baseline probability. Alarmists also tend to heavily
exaggerate the degree of internal change caused by Xi Jinping, which is
a factor that appears doubtful when aggregating forecasts.

Moreover, even though available forecasts rate the risk of war as low,
they might still be overestimating it. This is because all these
forecasts are found in works that study military issues and political
attitudes. [But [other things matter]{.mark}, too. [Taiwan's]{.mark}
crucial [role in]{.mark} the [semiconductor]{.mark} industry is well
known, and destroying these supply chains [would cost]{.mark} the world
economy [trillions]{.mark} of dollars per year. On top of this comes the
effects of the [economic warfare]{.mark} that almost certainly [would
result]{.mark} if China attacked Taiwan. Most [estimates
conclude]{.mark} that such [consequences would hit China]{.mark} even
[harder]{.mark} than the West. It is hard to imagine that China does not
realize this or refuses to take it into account when weighing the
possibility of using force]{.underline}. This should further subtract
from the generally low level of risk estimated by the forecasts we have
reviewed.

All of this taken together suggests that [a [mix of]{.mark} military and
economic [deterrence, together with a]{.mark} responsibly
[managed]{.mark} China-U.S. [relationship]{.mark}, can be expected to
[effectively prevent conflict]{.mark}]{.underline} even in the future.
Most importantly, [discussions of a possible Taiwan conflict should
proceed from an understanding of the low baseline probability of such a
scenario, not from panic-driven "when-rather-than-if"
takes]{.underline}.

### AT: Bioweapons !\-\--1NC

#### Even [super-diseases]{.underline} can't cause extinction\-\--[human dispersal]{.underline} and [countermeasures]{.underline}. 

**Thorstad 23**, PhD, Assistant Professor of Philosophy at Vanderbilt
University. (David, 7-8-2023, \"Exaggerating the risks (Part 9: Biorisk
-- Grounds for doubt),\" Reflective altruism,
https://reflectivealtruism.com/2023/07/08/exaggerating-the-risks-part-9-biorisk-grounds-for-doubt/)

In the meantime, I want to give some initial reasons for skepticism
about existential biorisk. That is not to say that the bulk of the case
against existential biorisk rests on these reasons for skepticism -- it
rests, instead, on the inability of effective altruists to provide
plausible arguments in support of their risk estimates. But it does seem
appropriate to begin by saying why [[many are skeptical of existential
biorisk]{.underline}]{.mark} claims.

3\. The difficulty of the problem

The main reason why scientists and policymakers are skeptical of
existential biorisk is that [it is terribly hard to engineer a pandemic
that kills everyone]{.underline}.

First, [[you would need to reach everyone]{.underline}]{.mark}. [The
virus would have to be transmitted to the most rural and isolated
corners]{.underline} of the earth; to [[antarctic]{.underline}]{.mark}
research [[stations]{.underline}]{.mark}; to [[ships]{.mark} at
sea]{.underline}, [including [nuclear
sub]{.mark}]{.underline}marine[[s]{.underline}]{.mark} on uncharted,
long-term and isolated paths; to [doomsday [preppers]{.mark}
in]{.underline} their [bunkers]{.underline}; to hermits and
[[uncontacted tribes]{.underline}]{.mark}; to [[astronauts]{.mark} in
space]{.underline}; to each new child born every second; [to island
nations]{.underline}; and so on. And you would need a transmission
mechanism that could spread the virus this far without being detected:
otherwise, [those with the means would be whisked away to
safety]{.underline} and might well survive.

Second, [[you would need a virus that was]{.mark} virtually
[undetectable]{.mark} until all]{.underline}, or [nearly all humans had
been infected]{.underline}. That conflicts in a stark way with the goal
of producing a virus that is 100% lethal, since lethal viruses tend to
leave a trace as they spread through a population.

Third, [you would need a virus that is unprecedentedly
infectious]{.underline}. [[The virus would need to be]{.mark} capable of
being [transmitted]{.mark}, without fail]{.underline}, [[to every
human]{.underline}]{.mark} being [[on the planet]{.underline}]{.mark},
in sufficient quantities to actually make them sick. [It would need to
[avoid respirators]{.mark}]{.underline} and other forms of protective
equipment. [[And]{.mark} it would have to [maintain]{.mark} its
[transmissibility through]{.mark}out many generations]{.underline} of
[[mutation]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Fourth, [you would need a virus that is unprecedentedly
lethal]{.underline}, [[killing not]{.underline}]{.mark} 90%,
[[99%]{.underline}]{.mark}, or 99.999% [[but]{.underline}]{.mark}
effectively [[all]{.underline}]{.mark} of those infected by it, [[no
matter their]{.mark} age, health, or [genetic
makeup]{.mark}]{.underline}. This lethality would need to be preserved
even against the best medical treatments, including quite possibly
vaccines or synthesized antibodies. And it would have to be maintained
throughout many generations of mutation despite selective pressures
towards less lethal variants.

Fifth, [[you would need to]{.underline}]{.mark} find a way to
[[evade]{.mark} basic public health measures such as masking and social
[distancing]{.mark}. This isn't as easy as it sounds. [How do you
transmit a virus to someone who doesn't leave their
house?]{.mark}]{.underline}

### AT: Weather !\-\--1NC

#### China won't deploy geoengineering. 

**Young 23**, Researcher at the University of Leeds (Danielle N. Young,
2023, "Considering stratospheric aerosol injections beyond an
environmental frame: The intelligible 'emergency' techno-fix and
preemptive security," European Journal of International Security, Vol.
8, pp. 262--280, University of Kansas Libraries, Pro Quest)

China, and to a lesser extent, India are frequently mentioned as actors
who may be interested in SAI use, unilateral or otherwise due to their
size, economic and military capacity, and relative vulnerability to
climate change.117 Although India theoretically has incentive to engage
in unilateral SAI due to adverse climate impacts, research indicates
that SAI will impact monsoon and rainfall patterns particularly
important to the country and which make the prospect of SAI much less
appealing to India than it may be for China.118 China is concerned about
climate change vulnerabilities and maintaining its development
trajectory, which may make SAI an appealing intervention to extend the
window for bringing down reliance on fossil fuels for growth.119
[[China]{.underline}]{.mark} also [[has a history
of]{.underline}]{.mark} engaging in large-scale engineering projects and
[[weather modification]{.underline}]{.mark} that may arguably make it
more comfortable with shifting into SAI deployment.120
[[However]{.mark}, China [has shown]{.mark} far [more interest
in]{.mark}]{.underline} CDR geoengineering [strategies like
[afforestation and]{.mark}]{.underline} some [[reluctance to explore
SRM]{.mark} strategies]{.underline},121 [[in addition to]{.mark} complex
geo[political challenges]{.mark} it would face [if it engaged]{.mark} in
SAI, particularly [with India and Australia]{.mark}]{.underline}.122
[[China]{.mark} appears to [have enough]{.mark} internal
[agreement]{.mark} that [climate]{.mark} change [is a]{.mark} serious
[problem]{.mark} for it [to mobilise]{.mark}]{.underline} political and
scientific efforts to [pursue]{.underline} longer-term, less immediately
[[impactful but]{.mark} also [less dangerous]{.mark} climate
[interventions]{.mark}]{.underline}.123 It is certainly not impossible
that China may engage in unilateral SAI in the event of a serious
environmental disaster, and it fits the profile sketched above of an
economically and militarily powerful country that may be able to evade
or withstand sanctions if it acted preemptively and unilaterally.
However, there is also a strong case to be made that the US is the actor
more likely to engage in unilateral geoengineering when broader
considerations beyond perceived environmental vulnerability are taken
into account.

#### States will cooperate, not fight, over geoengineering. 

**Horton 11**, PhD, Research Director of Geoengineering at Harvard.
(Joshua B., May 2011, "Geoengineering and the Myth of Unilateralism:
Pressures and Prospects for International Cooperation," Stanford Journal
of Law, Science & Policy Vol. IV, p. 65-67,
https://law.stanford.edu/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/horton.pdf)

Viewed in this way, [[the]{.mark} conventional [tendency to hear nuclear
echoes in]{.mark}]{.underline} contemporary [debates about
[geoengineering is turned on its head]{.mark}]{.underline}. The
governance challenges associated with SAI resemble the challenges
associated with arms control much more than the difficulties plaguing
nonproliferation efforts. With SAI and arms control, [[national
interests favor mutual accommodation]{.mark} and
coordination]{.underline}. With emissions mitigation and
nonproliferation, by contrast, national interests tend to diverge from
the collective interest, and hence international agreements are more
prone to breakdown. Disputes between nuclear weapons states and
non-nuclear weapons states are mirrored by debates between Annex I and
Annex II countries. [It is not]{.underline} SAI
[geoengineering]{.underline}, [but]{.underline} rather [mitigation
policy that is subject to]{.underline} the types of [malign
incentives]{.underline} typical of nuclear nonproliferation. Decades of
unsuccessful attempts to reduce global carbon emissions underscore this
similarity and explain the need to consider geoengineering in the first
place.

V. PROMOTING MULTILATERALISM

The benign problem structure underlying the question of SAI deployment
can be addressed through the use of a portfolio of tactics known to
political scientists as "international management theory." 44 Employing
these instruments facilitates the achievement of multilateral
coordination and effective international governance. By
[[using]{.underline}]{.mark} these [[policy levers]{.underline}]{.mark},
interested parties are able to [[overcome]{.underline}]{.mark} precisely
those kinds of [[obstacles]{.mark} that currently impede global
coop]{.underline}eration on SAI. International management theorists cite
three strategies as key to solving interdependence problems.
[Persuasion]{.underline} helps redefine national interests so that
states favor international cooperation. [Pressure]{.underline} brings
national policies into alignment with harmonious interests. Finally,
[provision of assistance]{.underline} enables states to pursue policies
that are compliant. Together, these "three Ps" [are the main instruments
by which noncompliance is ameliorated and governance regimes are made
successful]{.underline}. 45

[[In]{.mark} the case of [free trade]{.mark}]{.underline}, for example,
[[the international community]{.underline}]{.mark} has [systematically
[dismantled trade barriers through]{.mark}]{.underline} a process of
[[negotiation]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[pressure]{.underline}]{.mark},
[[and dispute resolution]{.underline}]{.mark} conducted in successive
rounds of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) and World
Trade Organization (WTO). 46 The problem of littoral state
territoriality and control has been largely resolved through the
Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ) construct developed and institutionalized
under the United Nations Law of the Sea (UNCLOS). 47 As noted above,
numerous arms control agreements based on explicit bargains and robust
monitoring and verification have mitigated the risk of a nuclear arms
race. Stratospheric ozone depletion has been arrested through interstate
learning, regulation, and capacity assistance in the context of the
Montreal Protocol. 48

In the case of SAI, [the strategy required to achieve
cooperative]{.underline}, [multilateral implementation of climate
intervention technologies is persuasion]{.underline}. [Persuasion
consists of efforts to alter conceptions of interest so that actors are
motivated to behave in ways that support successful
outcomes]{.underline}. Learning may be central to the process of
redefining interests. The objects of persuasion may include not only
governments, but also private firms, which are often the ultimate
targets of international regulation, as well as large societal groups
whose beliefs and preferences can prove critical to regime performance.
Implicit in arguments about the efficacy of persuasion as a
problem-solving strategy is a conception of interests as constructed,
subjective, and amenable to change.

In order to move toward deployment, [[major powers]{.underline}]{.mark}
must [[regard geoengineering as serving their]{.mark} national
[interest]{.mark}]{.underline}. In the case of stratospheric aerosols,
leading states must come to view injections as a necessary complement to
carbon reductions. Without consensus on the need for intervention, there
is little value in pressing for more robust action. And, capacity does
not present any serious obstacles. Indeed, an abundance of capacity in
the form of available technologies such as aerosol injections represents
a key structural imperative supporting global collective action, as it
increases the likelihood of discordant deployments and mutual
interference.

Persuading governments to rethink their interests and recast their
policies is, of course, a daunting challenge. National interests may be
malleable, but they are typically entrenched and taken for granted. 49
Although knowledge of geoengineering remains limited, and international
opinion is inchoate, there exists unmistakable latent hostility to
climate engineering in most national governments. 50 Opposition may
become further entrenched as the variable distributional effects of
climate change become clearer. 51 For example, Russia may prefer the
improved agricultural productivity and greater accessibility linked to
global warming to previously less hospitable conditions across the bulk
of its landmass. 52

However, [[such positions may be more amenable to change]{.mark} than
commonly supposed]{.underline}. At [a]{.underline}n April 2009
[geoengineering workshop in Lisbon]{.underline} held under the auspices
of the International Risk Governance Council (IRGC), [[representatives
from the]{.underline}]{.mark} United States
([[U.S.]{.underline}]{.mark}), Canada, European Union
([[E.U]{.mark}.]{.underline}) member states,
[[Russia]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[China]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[and
India]{.underline}]{.mark} [all [expressed]{.mark}]{.underline} some
degree of [[openness to]{.underline}]{.mark} the goals of [[climate
intervention]{.underline}]{.mark}. 53 Significantly, [the world's first
aerosol field trial was conducted in Russia]{.underline} in 2009, 54 led
by scientists with very close links to Russian government officials
including Prime Minister Vladimir Putin.55 [China engaged in weather
modification while hosting the]{.underline} 2008 Summer
[Olympics]{.underline}.56 Russia, China, and Japan all raised concerns
about the wisdom of the recent CBD moratorium. 57

However, [contrary to widespread fears, [a]{.mark}nother [Sino-Indian

#### Deterrence is stable internationally\-\--no risk of accidents or breakdowns. 

**Tertrais 11**, Ph.D., and M.A., Public Law, University of Paris (Bruno
Tertrais, Fall 2011, "In Defense of Deterrence: The Relevence, Morality,
and Cost-Effectiveness of Nuclear Weapons," Proliferation Papers,
Security Studies Center,
https://www.ifri.org/sites/default/files/atoms/files/pp39tertrais.pdf)

[[A]{.mark}nother [potential cost of]{.mark} nuclear [deterrence
is]{.mark} the [risk of miscalc]{.mark}ulation [or
accident]{.mark}]{.underline}. The risk of accidental nuclear war was
the subject of numerous reflections and studies during the Cold war.
More recently, a school of thought [embodied by the works of Scott Sagan
and Bruce Blair]{.underline} has put the emphasis on the risks inherent
to complex systems 61 and organizations such as those which manage
nuclear weapons.

Finally, as recalled above, one of the motivations for the \"abolition\"
of nuclear weapons is the risk of nuclear terrorism.

[It remains a fact, [however]{.mark}, that [no]{.mark} nuclear
[explosion has taken place in]{.mark} nearly [70
years]{.mark}]{.underline} (other than nuclear tests), and that, for
what is publicly known, [there not been either an accidental or
unauthorized launch]{.underline}, a weapon stolen, or a serious weapon
accident. [The [procedures]{.mark} that [guarantee safety]{.mark} and
security were simple if not rudimentary during the Cold war, but they
[are]{.mark} much [more robust]{.mark} and effective
[today]{.mark}]{.underline} in Western countries, and for what is
publicly known, rather elaborate in most other nuclear-armed countries.
No system is infallible, and there may very well be one day a major
nuclear incident; but [the [probability]{.mark} that such an incident
would lead to the actual detonation of a nuclear weapon [seems]{.mark}
to be [vanishingly small]{.mark}]{.underline}. Likewise, the probability
of nuclear terrorism seems to be vastly exaggerated. 62

As far as [[deterrence]{.mark} itself]{.underline} is concerned, it
would be wrong to calculate its inherent risks as one does for complex
technological systems: it primarily [[rests on human reasoning]{.mark}
--- which itself is far from being infallible, [but]{.mark}]{.underline}
as Robert Jervis says, [[it does not take a lot]{.mark} of rationality
for deterrence [to work]{.mark}]{.underline}. 63

[[To claim]{.mark} that \"[we have been lucky]{.mark} so
far\"]{.underline}, as have many analysts and politicians --- from
Robert McNamara to the Gang of Four --- [[is]{.mark} either metaphorical
or [unverifiable. By contrast]{.mark}]{.underline}, as explained below,
[[statistical studies]{.mark} have [shown]{.mark} that the [possession
of nuclear weapons]{.mark} significantly [reduced]{.mark} the
[probability of war]{.mark} among two countries]{.underline}. Kenneth
Waltz does not exaggerate when he claims that \"[the [probability
of]{.mark} major [war]{.mark} among states having nuclear weapons
[approaches zero]{.mark}]{.underline}\".64

Nuclear pessimism has a long lineage of authors who have been proven
wrong. In 1960, C. P. Snow wrote that if a dozen new countries were to
build nuclear weapons, the risk of a nuclear explosion in the next
decade would be a "mathematical certainty."65 In 1973, Fred Iklé, one of
the most brilliant American minds of the Cold war, who could not see any
other explanation for non-use than mere luck, predicted that nuclear
deterrence would probably fail before the end of the 20th century. 66
[There is no reason to take seriously the allegedly scientific
previsions made over the past few years, such as that of Martin Hellman
(1% risk of failure per year)]{.underline} or that made --- with less
intellectual pomp --- by the Scientific American magazine (one chance
out of 30 for the current decade). 67

The risk of nuclear escalation --- The risk of escalation has to exist
if deterrence is to be operative. But [[if one sets aside]{.mark} the
[Cuban]{.mark} Missile [Crisis]{.mark} (1962)]{.underline} for the
Soviet Union and perhaps, to some extent, the Yom Kippur War (1973) for
Israel, [there does [not]{.mark} seem to be [any example when]{.mark}
nuclear [weapons have been]{.mark} really [\"close\" to being
employed]{.mark}: neither [in Korea]{.mark} (1950), nor at
[Dien-Bien-Phu]{.mark} (1954), nor in the [Formosa]{.mark} Straits
(1954-1955 and 1957-1958); neither during the second [Berlin]{.mark}
crisis (1961), nor during the battle of [Khe Sanh]{.mark} (1968), the
[Ussuri River]{.mark} crisis (1969), the US/[No]{.mark}rth
[Ko]{.mark}rea tensions (1969), [the \"madman\"]{.mark} nuclear
[alert]{.mark} (1969) 68 or the [South Asia]{.mark} war (1971). Likewise
for the [Able Archer]{.mark} incident (1983), the [Gulf War]{.mark}
(1991), [or]{.mark} the South [Asian crises of]{.mark} 19[90]{.mark}
19[99 and 2002]{.mark}]{.underline}. 69 To envision the possible use of
nuclear weapons, discuss it with one\'s advisers, seriously consider it
if the crisis was to worsen, possibly make it known publicly (and/or put
forces on a higher state of alert), have it planned by military staffs
is one thing. To have \"the finger on the button\" and be on the verge
of ordering a nuclear strike is quite another. We will never know if
nuclear weapons would have been used if one of these crises had further
escalated. But they showed that with very few exceptions, [the highest
political authorities --- of various types of regimes and personality
--- have been extraordinarily prudent regarding their
use]{.underline}.70 [Most [exercises and wargames showed]{.mark} that
[possessors]{.mark} of nuclear weapons [were]{.mark} extraordinarily
[reluctant to]{.mark} engage in massive nuclear
[strike]{.mark}s.\"]{.underline} George Quester, one of the most subtle
American analysts of deterrence theory, considers, for instance ---
after a rigorous analysis of the early days of the Cold war --- that
ethical motivations were paramount to explain the absence of any US
nuclear use when it was in a situation of monopoly. 72 Hence the idea of
a \"nuclear taboo\" proposed by Nina Tannenwald for the United States or

in the consolidation of this taboo.]{.underline} 74

The risk of inadvertent nuclear war --- As for [the risk of
\"inadvertent\" nuclear war]{.underline} (a deliberate nuclear strike
because of a false alarm), this [is hardly a credible scenario. Multiple
false alarms took place during the Cold war]{.underline}. But contrary
to what some journalists and novelists may believe, there is no reason
to think that a US or Russian president has ever been close to launching
nuclear weapons due to a mere alert. One example frequently cited ---
which ranks high in the mythology of nuclear disarmament activists ---
is that of the 1995 Norwegian sounding rocket launch; the Russian early
warning system, at that time in very poor condition, had signaled that
it might be a missile.75 President Yeltsin had been summoned, and the
Cheget (the nuclear \"briefcase\") had reportedly been presented to him.
It is possible and even likely that Russia has a \"launch-on-warning\"
posture. But can one seriously believe that Moscow would have launched a
nuclear attack (against whom?) just because an unknown object had been
launched from Norway, and even before it was ascertained whether the
object was going to reach Russian territory (which was not the case)? As
for the Cold war false alarms --- there were several in the United
States in the 1980s, including because of [[software glitches]{.mark}
--- they [never led a]{.mark} US [President to]{.mark} envision a
nuclear [strike]{.mark}]{.underline}. In the United States, [[an
alert]{.mark} regarding a possible nuclear attack [has to be confirmed
by two different]{.mark} types of [sensors]{.mark} (dual phenomenology);
[a threat]{.mark} assessment [conference then]{.mark} would [decide
if]{.mark} political [authorities should be
contacted]{.mark}.]{.underline}

The risk of accidental or unauthorized use --- Likewise, the risk of an
\"accidental\" (non-deliberate) or \"unauthorized\" strike is
considerably exaggerated by disarmament activists. In most if not all
countries, to be launched, [nuclear forces have to receive a series of
complex instructions with multiple verifications]{.underline}. As
recalled by the commander of USSTRATCOM, General Chilton, [US missiles
are not on a \"hair-trigger alert\" posture: they are \"in the
holster]{.underline}. The nuclear forces of the five NPT- recognized
Nuclear-Weapon States have been detargeted, and Asian nuclear warheads
are reportedly separated from their launchers. [Such procedures have
been devised partly so that catastrophist fiction scenarios]{.underline}
--- which were, it should be said, much more credible until at least the
1960s --- [could not materialize]{.underline}. Serious incidents
regarding the custody of nuclear forces have been reported, but none
that ever posed the gravest risk. An American author has interestingly
suggested that since 1945, [[the]{.mark} tens of [thousands]{.mark} of
persons [that have had]{.mark} the [charge]{.mark}, at one level or
another, [of nuclear weapons]{.mark} \"must [have taken]{.mark} much
[greater care than]{.mark} is taken [in any other situation involving
human agents and complex mechanical systems]{.mark}."]{.underline}77

### AT: Foreign Nuclear Failures !\-\--2NC

**[States are rational]{.underline}, especially when the stakes are
high.**

**Mearsheimer 23**, \*PhD, Distinguished Service Professor in the
Political Science Department at the University of Chicago. \*\*PhD,
Professor of Political Science at the University of Notre Dame. (\*John
and \*\*Sebastian Rosato, *How States Think: The Rationality of Foreign
Policy*, Yale University Press, p. 11-12)

Assessing Strategic Rationality in World Politics

Once rationality has been defined, we can assess whether states are
rational actors. In essence, there are two positions on the matter. [We
argue that [states are routinely rational]{.mark}]{.underline}, while
political psychologists claim they are routinely nonrational. Analyses
of [both sets of arguments, as well as the historical record,
reveal]{.underline} that [rationality is commonplace]{.underline} in
international politics.

Routine Rationality

Given our definition of rationality as credible theories plus
deliberation, we find that generally speaking, [states are rational
actors]{.underline}. [Individual policymakers]{.underline} typically
[employ credible theories to inform their understanding of international
politics and decisions]{.underline} about the issues at hand [and
deliberate among themselves to formulate strategies]{.underline} for
reaching their goals.

Our analysis of the historical record focuses on a series of prominent
cases in which great powers formulated grand strategies and managed
crises. We examine instances in which those states are said to have
thought and acted in a nonrational fashion. The reason is simple: if the
great powers were rational in these cases of alleged nonrationality,
then they are likely to have been rational at most other times as well.
We have not, of course, analyzed the entire historical record. That is
impossible as there are innumerable cases of states making foreign
policy decisions and scant evidence on many of them. Nevertheless, we
believe our approach goes some way toward addressing the problem.

It is important to note that [in many of the cases we examine, the
chosen policy failed]{.underline}, sometimes disastrously. [This does
not mean that the state in question was nonrational]{.underline}.
[[There is a]{.mark} crucial conceptual [distinction between process and
outcomes]{.mark}, and [rationality is about the former]{.mark}, not the
latter]{.underline}. [Rational states seek to make sense of the world
and systematically consider the strategies available to them. [It does
not follow]{.mark} [that]{.mark} the policies [they]{.mark} choose [will
be successful]{.mark}]{.underline}. States can be committed to
theory-driven deliberation yet fail to reach their desired outcome
because of some exogenous constraint or unforeseen circumstance. By the
same token, there are several reasons---such as chance or overwhelming
superiority---why nonrational states may achieve their objectives. To
sum up, states can be rational and unsuccessful as well as nonrational
and successful. It therefore makes little sense to equate rationality
with outcomes. Nevertheless, a state that pursues a rational strategy is
more likely to succeed than fail since it has a good understanding of
international politics and has carefully pondered how to proceed.

None of this is to say that rationality is ubiquitous in international
politics. Indeed, we identify a number of instances of nonrationality,
where states either failed to deliberate, failed to base their policy on
a credible theory, or both.

[[There is a simple explanation]{.mark} for why states routinely think
and act rationally when making foreign policy]{.underline}.
[[International politics is]{.underline}]{.mark} a
[[dangerous]{.underline}]{.mark} business. [[States]{.mark}
[operate]{.mark} in a system [where there is no higher authority to
protect them]{.mark}]{.underline} and where other states can and may
want to do them grave harm. Consequently, [[they have a strong interest
in finding the best strategies to address]{.mark} the [problems]{.mark}
they confront]{.underline}. [[This leads]{.mark} individual
[policymakers to employ credible theories]{.mark} to make sense of the
world and decide what to do]{.underline}, [[as well as]{.mark} to
[deliberate among themselves to settle on a strategy]{.mark} for moving
forward]{.underline}. This is not to deny that [[states will sometimes
think]{.underline}]{.mark} and act [[nonrationally]{.underline}]{.mark}
when devising grand strategies or navigating crises.
[[But]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[high costs of failure mean]{.mark} that
[such cases are]{.mark}]{.underline} likely to be
[[uncommon]{.underline}]{.mark}.

### 

### AT: Lasers !\-\--1NC

#### Laser defense fails and makes conflict [more]{.underline}, not less, [likely]{.underline}. 

**Fedasiuk and Reif 18**, \*China Technology Policy Advisor at the U.S.
Department of State, M.A., Security Studies, Georgetown University,
\*\*Director for Disarmament and Threat Reduction Policy, Arms Control
Association (\*Ryan Fedasiuk, \*\*Kingston Reif, May 14, 2018, "Reasons
to Doubt Laser Missile Defense," Arms Control Association,
https://www.armscontrol.org/blog/2018-05-14/reasons-doubt-laser-missile-defense)

However, if the technology proves successful, the agency will run into
problems with beam quality. [As [a laser]{.mark} travels]{.underline} to
its target, "[it [encounters atmospheric effects that [distort the
beam](https://fas.org/sgp/crs/weapons/R41526.pdf)]{.mark} and cause it
to lose its focus]{.underline}." Particles in the atmosphere such as
[[water]{.mark} vapor, sand, [dust]{.mark}, salt, [and pollution]{.mark}
can [all absorb]{.mark} or refract a laser's
[energy]{.mark}]{.underline}, and [thermal
blooming](http://spie.org/Publications/Book/452443?SSO=1) is [of
particular concern in high-power laser weapons]{.underline}.

Tactical Considerations: Even if every technological barrier was
successfully conquered, though, [it's [far from clear]{.mark} that
[laser weapons would be]{.mark} an [effective]{.mark} form of [missile
defense]{.mark}]{.underline}. This is because lasers, unlike kinetic
interceptors, face limitations that are tactically insurmountable no
matter how advanced technology becomes:

• First, [a laser can only stop a missile if it generates enough
energy]{.underline} to cut into its electronics package. By adding [a
[harder, thicker]{.mark} layer of outside [shielding]{.mark},
adversaries [can strengthen]{.mark} a [missile's
"skin"]{.mark}]{.underline} and [prevent
it](https://breakingdefense.com/2014/05/the-limits-of-lasers-missile-defense-at-speed-of-light/) from
being disabled.

• Second, [lasers [can only]{.mark} focus [on one]{.mark} target [at a
time]{.mark}. An [adversary could]{.mark}]{.underline} simply [[launch a
salvo]{.mark} attack [to oversaturate]{.mark} laser
defenses]{.underline}.

• Third, laser weapons [can only destroy targets in [their line of
sight]{.mark}]{.underline}. This means most systems would be [[unable to
target low-flying]{.mark} cruise [missiles]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Moreover, line-of-sight systems are restricted by [the [curvature of the
earth]{.mark} and risk [let]{.mark}ting [targets get away [over the
horizon](https://fas.org/sgp/crs/weapons/R44175.pdf)]{.mark}]{.underline}---a
limitation not faced by heat-seeking kinetic interceptors.

There are also platform-specific limitations unique to lasers:

• Air-, ground-, and sea-based platforms [need to be
close](https://www.nuclearfiles.org/menu/key-issues/missile-defense/basics/general_principles.htm) to
the target's launch site to achieve boost-phase missile interception.
But perpetually keeping laser weapons near prospective launch sites is
financially unsustainable, and if the platform is manned, it is risky
for military personnel.

• Space-based platforms [[may]{.mark} accidentally [[damage]{.mark} or
destroy](https://www.ucsusa.org/sites/default/files/legacy/assets/documents/nwgs/slr_in_china.pdf) nearby
[satellites, risking escalation in a conflict]{.mark} or inhibiting U.S.
reconnaissance operations]{.underline}.

Despite [the many pitfalls of directed energy weapons]{.underline}, it
is unlikely that the Missile Defense Agency will soon relinquish the
chase for laser defense. But one thing is certain: the road ahead will
be difficult and costly.

#### It cannot solve in time\-\--takes 15 years. 

**Hitchens 22**, Writer, Breaking Defense, citing a study by the
American Physical Society, former Research Associate, University of
Maryland Center for International Strategic Studies (Theresa Hitchens,
February 22, 2022, "No US missile defense system proven capable against
'realistic' ICBM threats: Study," Breaking Defense,
https://breakingdefense.com/2022/02/no-us-missile-defense-system-proven-capable-against-realistic-icbm-threats-study/)

The [[study]{.underline}]{.mark} by the American Physical Society (APS)
[[examined]{.mark} a [hypothetical No]{.mark}rth [Ko]{.mark}rean [strike
and]{.mark}]{.underline} current [missile [defense]{.mark}]{.underline}

well as more futuristic options in development, like [[directed energy
weapons]{.underline}]{.mark} and space-based interceptors. [[It
found]{.mark} today's [capabilities inadequate and future systems

a [small number of No]{.mark}rth [Ko]{.mark}rean

### AT: Lasers !\-\--2NC

#### Boost-phase fails and is escalatory. 

**Hollings 23**, Military Reporter, the National Interest, former United
States Marine, citing DoD power figures and laser reports (Alex
Hollings, October 5, 2023, "Lasers Could Be Helpless Against Hypersonic
Weapons," National Interest,
https://nationalinterest.org/blog/buzz/lasers-could-be-helpless-against-hypersonic-weapons-206878)

In order to stop a hypersonic missile from reaching its target, [the
[DoD]{.mark} estimates that they'd [need at least]{.mark} a 1 megawatt
(or [1,000 kilo]{.mark}watt) [laser]{.mark}. That's [more than three
times]{.mark} the power output of [today's most advanced]{.mark}
tactical laser [system]{.mark}... but [even such]{.mark} a laser
[would]{.mark} likely [struggle to burn]{.mark} through [the nosecone of
a]{.mark} hypersonic [missile]{.mark}]{.underline}. After all, [these
[weapons are designed to withstand]{.mark} temperatures in excess of
[1,700 degrees]{.mark}]{.underline}. 

And as the Navy has pointed out, [[it's cheap]{.mark}er [to
build]{.mark} missiles with more [heat shielding]{.mark}]{.underline}
than it is to field lasers with more power output -- so as laser
defenses become more commonplace, missiles will almost certainly be
better shielded and thus, less susceptible to laser engagement. 

[Line of Sight]{.underline}

Unlike interceptors, which can be launched at targets identified beyond
the horizon, the very nature of [[lasers limit]{.mark}s them to [line of
sight]{.mark}. This presents problems when the laser needs time to burn
through a target]{.underline}. Hypersonic weapons like [[China's DF-ZF
may be]{.mark} traveling at [2 miles per second]{.mark} or even faster,
meaning [there would be]{.mark} precious [little time to]{.mark}
actually [destroy]{.mark} the weapon]{.underline} by the time it
appeared in the lasers' line of sight. 

[Atmospheric Scattering]{.underline}

We tend to think of lasers as a thin beam of energy that continues
onward forever, but that's really not the case at all. [[Water]{.mark}
vapor, [sand]{.mark}, salt, [smoke]{.mark}, air [pollution]{.mark}, and
other substances found [in the atmosphere]{.mark} can [all]{.mark} have
a [scatter]{.mark}ing effect on [laser beams]{.mark}]{.underline}. This
atmospheric [turbulence is a serious problem]{.underline} -- to the
point where [the [Pentagon]{.mark} currently [sees lasers as]{.mark} a
[viable]{.mark} weapon system [only]{.mark} at ranges of [less than a
mile]{.mark}. And even optimistic [projections for the]{.mark} near
[future still only]{.mark} think lasers will be [viable at]{.mark}
ranges of less than [five miles]{.mark}. ]{.underline}

### AT: I/L\-\--Hotlines\-\--1NC

#### China says no. 

**Morris and Marcrum 22**, \*Policy Analyst, RAND Corporation,
\*\*Colonel, United States Army, current student, United States Army War
College (\*Lyle Morris, \*\*Kyle Marcrum, July 27, 2022, "ANOTHER
"HOTLINE" WITH CHINA ISN'T THE ANSWER," Pacific Forum,
https://pacforum.org/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/PacNet41.2022.07.27-1.pdf)

However, [the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [and PRC hold different
assumptions]{.mark}]{.underline} and goals [[about
hotlines]{.underline}]{.mark}.

The United States views them as a tool to deconflict and de-escalate and
communicate between forces during a crisis. [The [PLA harbors]{.mark}
deep [suspicion about]{.mark} crisis [communications with]{.mark} the
[U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates, and [perceives US proposals]{.mark} for
new channels [as]{.mark} an [excuse to engage in provocative]{.mark}
military [activities]{.mark} near PRC claimed waters and
territory.]{.underline}

Beijing regards crisis communications as subservient to broader
political goals of "crisis management," which encompass exploiting
crises to its advantage and manipulating risk calculations. Hotlines are
not meant to resolve the crisis but to empower higher level organs
within the PRC to signal resolve, assign blame, and stall until Beijing
stakes out a position of maximum pressure and leverage over the United
States during negotiations.

This incompatibility has not stopped senior US defense officials from
holding out hope that hotlines and personal relationships with PLA
leaders might ease tensions. In his recent book, for instance, former US
Secretary of Defense Mark Esper says he could rapidly contact his
"counterpart," Minister of National Defense General Wei Fenghe, to clear
up misunderstandings.

Such [[hopes could be misplaced]{.mark}. Not only could hotlines fail to
solve crises, but they might also not perform as intended]{.underline}.
As Kurt Campbell, the Biden administration's National Security council
Coordinator for the Indo-Pacific, has said: [[hotlines]{.mark} tend to
"[ring endlessly in empty rooms]{.mark}."]{.underline}

### AT: I/L\-\--Hotlines\-\--2NC

#### China won't do hotlines.

**Mainprize 23**, Ph.D. Candidate, U.S. Foreign Policy, University of
Warwick (Ronan P. Mainprize, September 17, 2023, "Hotline Diplomacy is
No Cold War Relic," National Interest,
https://nationalinterest.org/feature/hotline-diplomacy-no-cold-war-relic-206813)

Hotlines exist between the United States and China at the executive and
military levels. Grounded in their experiences in the twentieth century,
various U.S. administrations have long viewed hotlines as a vital
diplomatic asset and have been motivated to use them
during [contemporary
crises](https://www.reuters.com/world/us-russia-have-used-deconfliction-line-once-so-far-during-ukraine-war-source-2022-11-28/),
either with China or other strategic competitors. Although [the
[C]{.mark}hinese [C]{.mark}ommunist [P]{.mark}arty leadership
[has]{.mark} so far [shown]{.mark} an [aversion to managing
escalation]{.mark} bilaterally with the United States,
frequently [leaving calls unanswered]{.mark} over the past few decades.
[Beijing ignored]{.mark} American [attempts]{.mark} to communicate
[during]{.mark} the [Tiananmen]{.mark} Square massacre in 1989. [Twelve
calls]{.mark} were made [by]{.mark} the [Bush]{.mark} administration
[during]{.mark} the [Hainan]{.mark} Island incident in 2001, with
[each]{.mark} of them similarly [being snubbed]{.mark}. The [same would
happen]{.mark} again [during]{.mark} numerous other episodes, and
[Xi]{.mark} Jinping and other Chinese policymakers [have
rebuffed]{.mark} [modest
[proposals]{.mark}](https://thehill.com/policy/international/4056697-xi-rejects-us-offer-to-set-up-military-crisis-hotline-blinken-says/)[ for]{.mark}
more active [hotline]{.mark} discussions.]{.underline}

Analysts have noted that [[Chinese]{.mark} officials [hold
suspicions]{.mark} over [why America]{.mark}n leaders [would
want]{.mark} to utilize [hotlines, viewing them as]{.mark} little other
than an [instrument]{.mark} for the United States [to]{.mark}
surreptitiously [normalize]{.mark} certain Indo-[Pacific]{.mark} region
[operations]{.mark}]{.underline}. More dangerously, they also believe
they can find strategic advantage in avoiding hotline conversations
and [the ambiguity this helps
create](https://open.spotify.com/episode/1Xm42G1VXat5PXNfIlDxox?si=26425479a4854eee).
But regardless of the differences of opinion, the Cold War shows
bilateral crisis management is still essential.

As [anxiety continues to mount over the fate of Taiwan and the South
China Sea]{.underline}, the United States must continue to pressure the
Communist Party leadership to come to the phone more promptly.
Complaining after the fact isn't enough. It should now be a significant
part of U.S. foreign policy to construct a more effective hotline
dialogue with Beijing. American officials must explain that the
ambiguity the Chinese leaders seek during a crisis is dangerous.

[With the rise of artificial intelligence and the [ubiquity of
disinformation](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/feb/28/china-spends-billions-on-pro-russia-disinformation-us-special-envoy-says),
serious miscalculations over specific naval movements or espionage
incidents have become more likely]{.underline}. A lack of communication
could result in a dangerous slide toward military or nuclear
confrontation. In a geopolitical climate where [atomic
threats](https://foreignpolicy.com/2023/09/06/putin-nuclear-war-ukraine-russia-biden-west-armageddon-psychology-influence-operations-disinformation-manipulation/) have
again begun to be bandied around, Beijing and Washington will find that
talking is the best medicine.

#### Low deficit threshold\-\--calls go to the top of the military and anyone [willing to pick up]{.underline} the phone would [be executed]{.underline}.

#### Hotlines fail\-\--China empirically refuses, they take forever, and the US needs to make concessions. 

**Spegele 23**, M.B.A., Columbia Business School (Brian Spegele, April
30, 2023, "China Balks at U.S. Push for Better Communications During
Crises," The Wall Street Journal,
https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-balks-at-u-s-push-for-better-communications-during-crises-3ed48ae6)

BEIJING---[[China is resisting]{.mark} a U.S. push to build]{.underline}
more-reliable [systems for [communicating in a crisis, raising]{.mark}
the [risk]{.mark} that [a miscalc]{.mark}ulation]{.underline} by either
side's military [[could spill into conflict]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Rarely since the Cold War have tensions between [two global
powers](https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-china-plunge-further-into-a-spiral-of-hostilities-b9e539c0?mod=article_inline) risen
to the levels that exist between the U.S. and China today. But unlike
the Soviets, who embraced crisis hotlines with Washington as a way to
defuse tensions, Beijing is resisting the establishment of new
communication channels. [[As Chinese]{.mark} officials [see it, hotlines
give]{.mark} the [U.S. cover to continue]{.mark} what they view as
provocative [military operations]{.mark} in China's
backyard.]{.underline}

The democratically self-governing island of Taiwan, which Beijing claims
as part of China, sits at the nexus of the challenge. Concern that a
heavy military presence around Taiwan could cause a mishap is a major
reason why Washington wants better communication lines with Beijing.
Chinese leaders see the U.S.'s increasing support for Taiwan as
undermining the foundations of trust needed to establish credible
communication between the two powers.

The seriousness of the situation has grown as American and Chinese
military jets and naval craft operate in proximity in [contested skies
and
waters](https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-jet-fighters-step-up-pressure-on-u-s-aircraft-over-south-china-sea-c2d1ac88?mod=article_inline).
The two militaries regularly brush up against each other as China seeks
to ward off the U.S. from operating in the South China Sea, most of
which China claims.

China's use of military drills to exert pressure on Taiwan raises the
risks of an accident. When China launched [military
exercises](https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinas-military-greets-return-of-taiwans-president-with-live-fire-drills-e6737c7c?mod=article_inline) around
Taiwan in April in response to a meeting between House Speaker Kevin
McCarthy (R., Calif.) and Taiwan's President Tsai Ing-wen in California,
a U.S. aircraft carrier was operating only a few hundred miles away.

"There is a significant reason for concern if the United States and
China stumble into a crisis over Taiwan," said Michael Swaine, a senior
research fellow at the Quincy Institute for Responsible Statecraft. "I'm
not convinced that all sides involved, including the Taiwanese, have
what it takes to ensure that the crisis would be resolved successfully."

The Biden administration says that it isn't seeking the sort of
confrontation with China that the U.S. had with the Soviet Union. For
one thing, the U.S. and China are far more interconnected economically
than the U.S. and Soviet Union were, despite the recent hostility and
efforts by both countries to make their economies less interdependent. 

[Business executives with
interests](https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-turns-to-back-channel-diplomacy-to-shore-up-u-s-ties-11669042014?mod=article_inline) in
stable U.S.-China ties have helped previously to smooth tensions between
the countries. With business connections severed by the pandemic slowly
being re-established, prospects for improved communication are rising.

Even so, senior U.S. officials are talking openly about taking a page
from the previous era and establishing hotlines. 

"We built those during the Cold War, and we think they are appropriate
now," said Kurt Campbell, the White House's coordinator for the
Indo-Pacific, at a think-tank event in March.

So far these efforts, which U.S. officials describe as [putting
guardrails around the relationship, haven't worked]{.underline}, Mr.
Campbell said.

[[China and]{.mark} the [U.S. already have]{.mark} some
[hotlines]{.mark}]{.underline} that connect their leaders, including a
military link established in 2008. A key problem is that [[China]{.mark}
sometimes [refuses to use]{.mark} this hotline [in]{.mark} moments of
[crisis, rendering it]{.mark} an [unreliable]{.mark} tool to manage
tensions]{.underline}. In addition, unlike with red phones in Hollywood
movies, [[meetings]{.mark} through the military hotline [can take
days]{.mark} or more [to set up]{.mark}.]{.underline} 

[[China declined to speak]{.mark} with U.S.]{.underline} Defense
Secretary Lloyd Austin in February [[after]{.mark} the [U.S.
shot]{.mark} down [a]{.mark}]{.underline} suspected [[Chinese]{.mark}
surveillance [balloon]{.mark}]{.underline} that had drifted across the
continental U.S. At the time, [[China said]{.mark} the [U.S. hadn't
created]{.mark} the right [conditions for dialogue. Calls for]{.mark}
better [communication ring hollow if Washington violates Beijing's most
sensitive areas of concern]{.mark}]{.underline} such as the "One China"
policy on Taiwan, said Xu Bu, a former senior Chinese diplomat who is
now president of the Foreign Ministry-affiliated China Institute of
International Studies.

**[AT: I/L\-\--AC\-\--1NC]{.underline}**

**Arms control fails\-\--adversaries [abuse]{.underline} the process.**

**Dodge 23**, PhD, research scholar at the National Institute for Public
Policy. (Michaela, 6-19-2023, \"Michaela Dodge, On Arms Control and Why
New START's Suspension Does Not Really Matter, No. 557,\" Nation
Institute for Public Policy,
https://nipp.org/information_series/michaela-dodge-on-arms-control-and-why-new-starts-suspension-does-not-really-matter-no-557-june-19-2023/)

[The U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates [ought not
mourn]{.underline} Russia's suspension of the New Strategic Arms
Reduction Treaty ([New START]{.underline}). [Claims]{.underline} that
[the treaty was]{.underline} "a key [stabilizing]{.underline} force in
U.S.-Russian [relations]{.underline} for more than a decade" [are
mistaken]{.underline}.\[1\] New START's demise will not make U.S.-Russia
relations any worse than they already are, thanks to Russia's aggressive
actions, including its full-scale invasion of Ukraine in February
2022.\[2\] That is because arms races, and by extension arms control,
are a reflection of politics, as the eminent strategist Colin Gray
famously postulated.\[3\] His fundamental point is that [[because arms
control]{.mark} [reflects]{.mark} overarching political
[relations]{.mark}, arms [agreements]{.mark} with foes [are unlikely to
accomplish much]{.mark}]{.underline}, [and agreements with friends
are]{.underline} likely to be [superfluous]{.underline}. It is also why
Russia's New START suspension does not fundamentally alter the
relationship between the two countries; nor does it make the United
States and allies any less secure than when the treaty was intact.

[[Arms control has a history of failing]{.underline}]{.mark} to live up
to the arms controllers' high expectations. For example,
[[the]{.underline}]{.mark} 1972
[[A]{.underline}]{.mark}nti-[[B]{.underline}]{.mark}allistic
[[M]{.underline}]{.mark}issile Treaty [[led the Soviet Union]{.mark}
[to]{.mark}]{.underline} spend [[increase]{.underline}]{.mark}d
resources on [[offensive nuc]{.underline}]{.mark}lear
weapon[[s]{.underline}]{.mark}, contrary to what proponents of the
treaty argued would happen at the time.\[4\] Reaching an agreement can
hardly be considered a success when the agreement does not advance U.S.
national security interests or puts an adversary in a better position to
compete. [Even arms control that succeeds initially can fail when one
side unilaterally chooses to cheat]{.underline}. As Colin Gray observed
during the Cold War, "The political antagonism that generates the
objective need for alleviation via arms control---always assuming, again
fallaciously, that arms control could control---is the very reason why
arms control must fail...."\[5\] [[The]{.underline}]{.mark} 1987
[[I]{.underline}]{.mark}ntermediate-Range [[N]{.underline}]{.mark}uclear
[[F]{.underline}]{.mark}orces Treaty [[coincided with
a]{.underline}]{.mark}n unprecedented [[thaw]{.underline}]{.mark} in
bilateral relations and successfully eliminated an entire class of
ground-launched nuclear missiles---[[until Russia]{.underline}]{.mark}
blatantly [[cheated]{.underline}]{.mark} on it, leading the Trump
Administration to withdraw from the treaty in 2019. The first Strategic
Arms Limitation Talks ([[SALT I]{.underline}]{.mark}) in 1972
[[failed]{.mark} to result in meaningful nuclear
