**Nuclear cuts make arsenals vulnerable to [conventional
counterforcing]{.underline}\-\--nuclear war.**

**Lieber 19**, \*PhD, professor in the Center for Security Studies in
the School of Foreign Service and the Department of Government @
Georgetown. \*\*PhD, Professor of Government @ Dartmouth. (\*Keir A. and
\*\*Daryl G. Press, "Why Nuclear Disarmament and Strategic Stability Are
Incompatible," in *Nuclear Disarmament: A Critical Assessment*, pg.
54-55)

Conclusion

[In the past]{.underline}, nuclear [arms control could]{.underline}
arguably [pursue both strategic stability]{.underline} (by preserving
the survivability of retaliatory forces and thus reducing the incentives
for pre-emptive disarming strikes) [and smaller arsenals]{.underline}
(by cutting the numbers of weapons). [Such an approach is increasingly
dubious]{.underline} as a means of deterring nuclear conflict
[today]{.underline}. [A new era of tech]{.underline}nology
[means]{.underline} that [further [nuclear cuts
would]{.mark}]{.underline} likely [[undermine
deterrence]{.underline}]{.mark} -- [leading to a world without the
stalemating benefits of nuclear possession yet with all the]{.underline}
traditional [dangers]{.underline} and more.

For decades, [[engineers]{.mark} have toiled to [improve weapons
accuracy and remote sensing]{.mark} capabilities]{.underline}.
Meanwhile, arms negotiators have devised agreements to reduce nuclear
arsenals. Either endeavor -- improving weapons or cutting stockpiles --
can be defended as a policy for promoting strategic stability, but taken
together they are creating underappreciated vulnerabilities. Good
intentions aside, [[nuclear]{.mark} arms [cuts]{.mark} today effectively
[reduce the number of targets]{.mark} that [an attacker must destroy in
a disarming strike]{.mark}]{.underline}. [The danger of nuclear
reductions is [exacerbated by]{.mark} improvements in the nonnuclear
means of attacking nuclear forces]{.underline}, for example,
[through]{.underline} [precision [conventional
strike]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[missile defense]{.underline}]{.mark},
[[anti-sub]{.underline}]{.mark}marine [war]{.underline}fare, [[and
cyber]{.underline}]{.mark} operations.

Arms controllers may respond that one merely needs to restrict the
development of advanced counterforce systems, and then arms cuts will be
safe again. However, [the [tech]{.mark}]{.underline}nological
[[developments]{.mark} in accuracy and remote sensing [are so integral
to]{.mark} modern [conventional war]{.mark}fare]{.underline} --
[[and]{.mark} modern [society]{.mark}]{.underline} -- that
[[they]{.underline}]{.mark} [[would be]{.underline}]{.mark} nearly
[[impossible to halt]{.underline}]{.mark}, even if arms controllers
could come up with clever treaty designs.

The implications of the new era of counterforce extend beyond the wisdom
of future nuclear arms control. First, [if nuclear forces are becoming
easier to attack, then all else being equal, nuclear]{.underline}-armed
states [need to deploy more capable retaliatory arsenals]{.underline} to
counter the growing risks. Whether one believes that a deterrent force
must present potential attackers with "near-certain retaliation,"
"likely retaliation," or some other level of risk, improvements in
counterforce systems require that retaliatory forces adapt -- through
better capabilities, increased numbers, or both -- to maintain the same
level of deterrent threat. Furthermore, [the rapid rate of change in
counterforce technologies increases uncertainty about adversaries'
future capabilities]{.underline} -- suggesting that countries will need
to retain diverse retaliatory forces as a hedge against adversary
breakthroughs.

Second, one should ask whether it is wise for the United States in
particular to continue improving its nuclear and non-nuclear
counterforce capabilities. On the one hand, improved counterforce
capabilities could be invaluable in a range of plausible scenarios --
particularly in a crisis or conventional war when an adversary threatens
or has begun to escalate. Improved offensive capabilities could help the
United States deter weak countries from initiating conventional
conflicts or from escalating in the midst of war. Enhanced counterforce
capabilities could also help protect US forces, allies, and the US
homeland from nuclear attack if a conventional war did escalate. On the
other hand, [better counterforce could be a source of danger: not only
might improved disarming strike capabilities -- in any country's hands
-- increase the temptation to attack]{.underline}, but [also [potential
victims]{.mark} of disarming strikes [will seek to escape]{.mark} their
[vulnerability]{.mark}]{.underline}, thereby possibly
[[triggering]{.underline}]{.mark} arms racing and [[incentives to strike
pre-emptively]{.underline}.]{.mark}

Ultimately, both views about the wisdom of enhancing counterforce
capabilities may be correct. The net benefit will therefore depend on
the particular case. For countries that perceive a highly malign threat
environment, face aggressive nuclear-armed adversaries, or have
ambitious foreign policy goals, the benefits of developing advanced
counterforce capabilities may outweigh the costs. For those countries
that face a benign environment and have more modest goals, however, the
secondary costs of enhancing counterforce may be too great. In either
case, however, policymakers and analysts need to reckon with the ways
that rapidly changing technologies are eroding the foundation of
deterrence and nuclear arms control. In a world where [[nuclear]{.mark}
arms [cuts and]{.mark}]{.underline} strategic [[stability are no longer
compatible]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[smaller arsenals are more
vulnerable]{.mark} arsenals]{.underline}.

**[Turn\-\--AC\-\--Space\-\--2NC]{.underline}**

#### China and Russia [pocket the agreement]{.underline} increasing [adversarial militarization]{.underline} AND hinder American [space projections]{.underline}.

**Bowman & Thompson 21**, \*the senior director of the Center on
Military and Political Power at the Foundation for Defense of
Democracies \*\*a U.S. Air Force major and visiting military analyst at
the Foundation for Defense of Democracies. (\*Bradley \*\*Jared,
3-31-2021, "Russia and China Seek to Tie America's Hands in Space,"
Foreign Policy,
https://foreignpolicy.com/2021/03/31/russia-china-space-war-treaty-demilitarization-satellites/#cookie_message_anchor)

Consider the actions of the United States' two great-power adversaries
when it comes to anti-satellite weapons. [[China and Russia]{.mark}
have [sprinted to develop]{.mark} and deploy both
[ground]{.mark}]{.underline}-based [[and space-based weapons
targeting]{.mark} satellites [while simultaneously pushing]{.mark} the
U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates [to sign a [treaty]{.mark}
banning such weapons]{.underline}.

To protect its vital space-based military capabilities---including
communications, intelligence, and missile defense satellites---and
effectively deter authoritarian aggression, [Washington should avoid
being drawn into suspect international treaties on space that [China and
Russia have no intention of honoring]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The Treaty on the Prevention of the Placement of Weapons in Outer Space
and of the Threat or Use of Force Against Outer Space Objects (PPWT),
which Beijing and Moscow have submitted at the United Nations, is a
perfect example. PPWT signatories commit "not to place any weapons in
outer space." It also says parties to the treaty may not "resort to the
threat or use of force against outer space objects" or engage in
activities "inconsistent" with the purpose of the treaty.

On the surface, that sounds innocuous. Who, after all, wants an arms
race in space?

The reality, however, is that [China and Russia are already racing to
field anti-satellite weapons and have been for quite some
time]{.underline}. "The space domain is competitive, congested, and
contested," Gen. James Dickinson, the head of U.S. Space
Command, said in January. "Our competitors, most notably China and
Russia, have militarized this domain."

Beijing already has an operational ground-based anti-satellite missile
capability. People's Liberation Army units are training with the
missiles, and the U.S. Defense Department believes Beijing "probably
intends to pursue additional \[anti-satellite\] weapons capable of
destroying satellites up to geosynchronous Earth orbit." That is where
America's most sensitive nuclear communication and missile defense
satellites orbit and keep watch.

Similarly, Moscow tested a ground-based anti-satellite weapon in
December that could destroy U.S. or allied satellites in orbit. That
attack capability augments a ground-based laser weapon that Russian
President Vladimir Putin heralded in 2018. In a moment of candor,
Russia's defense ministry admitted the system was designed to "fight
satellites."

To make matters worse, [both]{.underline} countries [are]{.underline}
also [working to deploy space-based]{.underline}---or so-called
"on-orbit"---[capabilities to attack satellites]{.underline}.

Meanwhile, at the United Nations and other international
forums, China and Russia are pushing the PPWT and advocating for a "no
first placement" resolution---saying all governments should commit not
to be the first to put weapons in space.

Yet more than two years ago, the U.S. Defense Intelligence
Agency noted that both China and Russia were already putting in space
capabilities that could be used as weapons. The PPWT would thus protect
their weapons while tying Washington's hands.

In a thinly veiled attempt to mask their intentions, the two countries
claim that their on-orbit capabilities are simply for peaceful
purposes---for assessing the condition of broken satellites and
conducting repairs as needed. This "dual-use" disguise permits Beijing
and Moscow to put into orbit ostensibly peaceful or commercial
capabilities that those countries can actually use to disable or destroy
U.S. military and intelligence satellites.

China, for example, has tested several so-called scavenger satellites,
which use grappling arms to capture other satellites. China has also
demonstrated the capability to maneuver a satellite around
the geosynchronous belt, allowing its satellites to sidle up to other
satellites in space.

Not to be outdone, Russia deployed a pair of "nesting doll" satellites
that shadowed a U.S. satellite in space. One Russian satellite birthed
another, with Russia's defense ministry claiming its purpose was to
assess the "technical condition of domestic satellites."

But later, the second satellite conducted a weapons test, firing what
appeared to be a space torpedo. The Kremlin never explained how a
fast-moving one-time projectile provided superior inspection benefits
compared with the other Russian satellite flying persistently nearby.

A well-crafted treaty that clearly defines acceptable and unacceptable
actions in space and includes tough and realistic inspection and
verification mechanisms could promote security and stability. But the
PPWT is decidedly not that kind of treaty.

For starters, the proposed [[treaty does not]{.mark} explicitly
[prohibit]{.mark} the ground-based anti-satellite [weapons that China
and Russia have]{.mark} already [fielded. Nor]{.mark}]{.underline} does
the proposed treaty [prevent the deployment of space-based weapons under
the [cloak of civilian]{.mark} or commercial
[capabilities]{.mark}]{.underline}. The PPWT also does not prohibit the
development, testing, or stockpiling of weapons on Earth that could be
quickly put into orbit.

Even if [these deficiencies]{.underline} were addressed, the PPWT
[[lacks]{.mark} any [verification]{.mark} plan [to ensure
compliance]{.mark}]{.underline}. Instead, the treaty calls for
"transparency and confidence-building measures" implemented on a
"voluntary basis." In other words, [Beijing and Moscow want the
U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates [to trust but never
verify]{.underline}.

But then again, Americans should not be surprised by the PPWT. Moscow
habitually seeks to use international arms control treaties to constrain
the United States while viewing treaty strictures as optional when they
become inconvenient or when the Kremlin sees an opportunity to seize a
military advantage.

For more than a decade before its demise in 2019, Moscow used the
Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty to constrain the United States
while the Kremlin produced, flight-tested, and fielded a ground-launched
intermediate-range cruise missile in direct contravention of the treaty.
Beijing, for its part, often exhibits an allergy to serious
international arms control treaties. The willingness of the Chinese
Communist Party to support the PPWT is, therefore, cause for some
additional reflection in Washington.

So instead of falling prey to China and Russia's PPWT trap, the United
States must urgently work with allies to improve the resilience and
redundancy of spaced-based military and intelligence capabilities.

Washington should also advance nascent efforts to establish rules of the
road in space. "There are really no norms of behavior in space," Gen.
John Raymond, the chief of space operations at U.S. Space
Force, said this month. "It's the wild, wild West."

In a notable and positive step, [the [U.N. G]{.mark}]{.underline}eneral
[[A]{.underline}]{.mark}ssembly [[passed]{.mark} a]{.underline}
British-introduced [[resolution]{.underline}]{.mark} in December that
seeks [[to establish]{.mark} "norms, rules and principles of responsible
[behaviours]{.mark}" in space]{.underline}, which could reduce the
chances for dangerous miscalculation.

[The vote was 164 in favor, including the U]{.underline}nited
[S]{.underline}tates---[and]{.underline} a mere [[12
opposed]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Any guesses regarding [[who voted no?]{.underline}]{.mark} You guessed
it: [[China]{.underline}]{.mark} and [[Russia]{.underline}]{.mark}. They
were joined by their friends [[Iran, North Korea, Syria, Venezuela, and
Cuba]{.underline}]{.mark}.

So much for a [Chinese and Russian]{.underline} desire to pursue
constructive and peaceful policies in space. Their [[duplicity
continues]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### More warrants:

#### 1. [DEFINITIONS]{.underline} elude agreements. That cause [loopholes]{.underline} AND [dual-use]{.underline} issues.

**Britt 22**, B.S. in International Relations and an M.A. in Security
Studies from Geogetown. He is now an analyst for Avascent, an aerospace
and defense firm. (Brian, 2-21-2022, "Arms Control in Outer Space won't
Work," The Space Review, https://www.thespacereview.com/article/4336/1)

Elusive definitions

[Treaties that ban or control weapons must define]{.underline} what,
exactly, they aim to ban or control. [Without an ASAT
definition]{.underline} that is simultaneously inclusive and precise,
[[international regimes]{.underline}]{.mark} preventing the development,
deployment, and use of ASATs [[are ineffective]{.mark}. Two obstacles
make constructing a]{.underline} useful [definition]{.underline} nearly
[impossible]{.underline}: weaponry diversity and the dual-use problem.

[The [variety of]{.mark} potential]{.underline} ASAT [[weaponry
presents]{.mark} a [problem]{.mark}]{.underline}. ASAT weapons are any
technology that can temporarily or permanently disable or destroy a
satellite's functionality. This means [[lasers]{.mark} and other
directed [energy]{.mark} weapons, air- and land-launched
[kinetic]{.mark} missiles, [cyber]{.mark} uplink and downlink attacks,
radiofrequency [jamming]{.mark} attacks, [attacks on ground]{.mark}
stations, [and maneuverable]{.mark} attack [satellites are all ASAT
weapons]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The diversity of ASAT weapons makes articulating a sufficiently
comprehensive and precise definition impossible. [[Any attempt]{.mark}
to do so [will]{.mark} inevitably [leave]{.mark} significant
[loopholes]{.mark} because each [technology exists in]{.mark} a
[separate domain defined by unique]{.mark} operational
[requirements]{.mark}, norms, and expectations that require specific
rules and regulations to control]{.underline}.

[Controlling these technologies is especially difficult given most have
legitimate, peaceful, or commonplace uses. [Satellites]{.mark} are
fragile]{.underline}. It takes little force to render them temporarily
or permanently ineffective. [When a target is [defined by fragility,
everything becomes a weapon, meaning]{.mark} innocent and [commonplace
technologies]{.mark} can [be weaponized]{.mark}]{.underline}. Remotely
operated repair satellites, for instance, are being developed to
revitalize failing satellites.\[5\] But the same capabilities used to
repair can be repurposed to destroy. Similarly, any satellite equipped
with a radiofrequency antenna necessary to receive signals can also emit
them with sufficient strength to jam the communications of nearby
satellites.\[6\] [This [dual-use]{.mark} problem [presents]{.mark} an
[obstacle for]{.mark}]{.underline} ASAT [[arms control]{.mark} by
pitting legitimate and peaceful operational freedom against national
security]{.underline}.

The PPWT defines a weapon as "any outer space object or its component
produced or converted to eliminate, damage or disrupt normal functioning
of objects in outer space."\[7\] But by the most literal interpretation
of this language, almost everything can be classified as an ASAT and
cannot be put into space. The PPWT does not even attempt to address ASAT
weapons that are deployed from the ground. [Effective
definitions]{.underline} elude the PPWT and will also [elude future arms
control attempts]{.underline}.

#### 2. [VERIFICATION]{.underline} is [impossible]{.underline}\-\--[technological]{.underline} AND [trust]{.underline} deficiencies.

**Britt 22**, B.S. in International Relations and an M.A. in Security
Studies from Geogetown. He is now an analyst for Avascent, an aerospace
and defense firm. (Brian, 2-21-2022, "Arms Control in Outer Space won't
Work," The Space Review, https://www.thespacereview.com/article/4336/1)

Verification problems

To identify and differentiate between compliance and disobedience, the
international community must be able to identify and characterize
objects in orbit. International arms control treaties cannot rely on
goodwill alone. Verification capabilities must therefore be an inherent
part of any agreement that restricts the use or deployment of weaponry.

But it won't be in outer space---[[no reliable verification
capabilities]{.underline}]{.mark} currently
[[exist]{.underline}]{.mark}. Once an asset is deployed in orbit,
[[it's]{.mark} remarkably [difficult to tell what it is and]{.mark} what
it is [capable of]{.mark} doing. [For non-physical]{.mark}
weapons]{.underline}, like cyber and jamming capabilities, [[there are
no means]{.mark} to verify compliance]{.underline}. Any attempt to ban
non-physical ASAT weapons would rely exclusively on trust. The PPWT
seeks to bar signatories from placing "any weapons in outer space."\[8\]
[[It's mere finger-wagging more than]{.mark} an [enforceable]{.mark}
rule without a means to verify compliance]{.underline}.

Advocates for ASAT arms control would disagree, pointing to modern space
situational awareness capabilities that can detect space launches and
track the orbital inclination of their payloads through space-based
infrared and infrasound monitoring systems.\[9\] Even if states can
track objects that are placed into orbit, "verifying the function of a
particular space object already in orbit is significantly more
difficult."\[10\] The "PAXSAT A" study demonstrated that uncovering the
functionality of satellites in orbit is possible by utilizing a
four-satellite constellation.\[11\] But this investigation method relies
on the ability of the constellation to position itself near the object
in question and the assumption that form follows function. Two problems
are presented here. First, [[we do not have]{.mark} the [infrastructure
to conduct]{.mark} on-orbit [investigations]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Second, the logic that form follows function is fallible. Because space
launches are expensive, advocates argue that the extra weight and cost
needed to obscure the true function of an ASAT weapon in orbit with
facade architecture is too expensive to be realistic. But [basing [arms
control]{.mark} agreements [on]{.mark} the [predicted frugality]{.mark}
of nation-states [seems illogical]{.mark}]{.underline}, at best.

Even with perfect launch monitoring capabilities, [significant unknowns
remain. Many space-related armaments are
[ground-based]{.mark}]{.underline}, meaning these weapons can be
developed and deployed outside of the scope of current ASAT monitoring
systems. Also, [for [non-physical]{.mark} weapons, verification is
simply impossible. No mechanism exists]{.underline}, for example, [to
verify compliance with moratoriums against developing or deploying
[cyberweapons or jamming]{.mark} capabilities]{.underline}.

In short, [effective [verification]{.mark} mechanisms [would be costly,
complicated, and ineffective]{.mark}. And without]{.underline}
verification capabilities, [useful arms control agreements are
hopeless]{.underline}.

#### 3. [ATTRIBUTION]{.underline} is [foundational]{.underline}, BUT [impossible]{.underline}.

**Britt 22**, B.S. in International Relations and an M.A. in Security
Studies from Geogetown. He is now an analyst for Avascent, an aerospace
and defense firm. (Brian, 2-21-2022, "Arms Control in Outer Space won't
Work," The Space Review, https://www.thespacereview.com/article/4336/1)

Attribution problems

Attribution, the ability to identify a violation and legitimately tie it
to an actor, is a key ingredient of arms control agreements. [The
[ability to attribute]{.mark} violations [is key to]{.mark} justifying
the [invoking]{.mark} of [punishments in accordance with]{.mark}
international treaty [law]{.mark}]{.underline}. Thus,
[[attribution]{.mark} capabilities [are]{.mark} the [foundation
of]{.mark}]{.underline} a deterrence [[mechanism that makes arms
control]{.underline}]{.mark} agreements [[attractive and
practical]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Attribution capabilities, or the lack thereof, present another problem
for ASAT arms control. Some forms of ASAT attacks are attributable.
Others are not. Non-physical threats, for instance, are particularly
difficult to tie actions to national actors.

In the event a [[laser]{.underline}]{.mark}, for example, is used
against a space asset, satellite operators would have a difficult time
identifying the actor responsible for the attack. In the harsh
environment of space, systems frequently fail without explanation.
Unless the targeted satellite is equipped with sensors that could
identify "a spike in thermal energy or sudden saturation of optical
sensors," there is no way to differentiate between a random satellite
failure and a malicious laser attack.\[12\] And even if such
capabilities exist, there is no guarantee one could attribute the
laser's use to a national actor.

[[Jamming]{.underline}]{.mark} attacks are similarly difficult to
attribute. Satellites use a narrow range of the electromagnetic spectrum
to communicate. The crowded nature of orbits today means it is common
for multiple space assets to use similar or identical frequencies and,
as a result, routinely unintentionally jam the communications of a
neighboring satellite.\[13\] Differentiating between intentional and
unintentional jamming is difficult, if not impossible. In this
environment, verification and compliance mechanisms are complicated to
construct.

Recent [[cyberattacks]{.underline}]{.mark} have laid bare the difficult,
lengthy, and uncertain process of attributing, much less identifying and
understanding, cyber incursions. The Russian SolarWinds hack
demonstrated a lack of an ability to definitively identify the scope and
duration of a cyber penetration.\[14\] Similar problems will
[[plague]{.mark} efforts to assert [arms control]{.mark}
regimes]{.underline} on cyber ASAT weapons.

**[Turn\-\--AC\-\--Conventional\-\--2NC]{.underline}**

**Russian nuclear weapon reductions threaten their
second-strike\-\--that's destabilizing.**

**Bronder 21**, PhD in military history, PhD in astrophysics, Lieutenant
Colonel in the USAF, recently Materiel Leader at the Air Force Technical
Applications Center, where he managed a large portfolio of nuclear
surveillance and treaty-monitoring programs (T. Justin, October 2021,
"Future Directions for Great Power Nuclear Arms Control: Policy Options
and National Security Implications", Center for the Study of Weapons of
Mass Destruction Occasional Paper, No. 13, pg. 41-42)

Approach 2: Long-Term Multilateral Reductions

Summary. Approach 2 would be difficult to achieve without a breakthrough
in international relations, but the proposed two-step process provides a
possible pathway. Yet without assuming the more benign security
environment required to make this approach a reality, the resulting
[[arms control]{.underline}]{.mark} outcomes [[would result in negative
changes]{.mark} across evaluated criteria]{.underline}. Specifically,
the vast [reductions in U.S. nuclear forces would present risks to
strategic stability and extended deterrence, while the specific focus on
nuclear arsenals may not adequately address new
technologies]{.underline} in a way that positively affects U.S.
competitive advantage.

Strategic Stability: Negative to Neutral. Approach 2 retains
U.S.-Russian stability while bringing China into an official
framework.92 Strategic exchange calculations show [[that a secure
second-strike]{.mark} capability [remains for both the
U]{.mark}]{.underline}nited [[S]{.underline}]{.mark}tates [[and
Russia]{.underline}]{.mark} with little relative change from the
percentage of arriving forces compared to today's New START--limited
regime (tables 14 and 15), supporting a neutral rating.
[[One]{.underline}]{.mark} possible [[destabilizing trend can be
considered under Russia]{.mark}'s worst-case scenario]{.underline}
(day-to-day forces and electing to "ride out" the attack under the 500
warhead limit), [[in which "only" 39 warheads would reach
targets]{.mark} in the U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates. At this
low number, [[Moscow could]{.mark} [argue]{.mark} that U.S. [BMD]{.mark}
systems [and]{.mark} conventional]{.underline} [[prompt-strike]{.mark}
capabilities [present a destabilizing]{.mark}]{.underline} "splendid"
[[first-strike capability]{.underline}]{.mark}.
[[The]{.underline}]{.mark} potential [[erosion of Russia's]{.mark}
[second-strike]{.mark} capability could]{.underline} also [[foster
a]{.mark} "[use]{.mark} th[em]{.mark} [or lose]{.mark} th[em]{.mark}"
scenario]{.underline} in a major crisis.
